Maxim: Whoever Owns a Thing Owns Its Necessities — The Mukatab's Case
Extracting a fiqh principle from the mukatab's limited capacity to give gifts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that ownership of a thing includes its necessities and apply it to the case of a mukatab's limited capacity to give charity.

Core Idea
Ownership of trade necessities is included in ownership of the trade itself.

This passage introduces a key Hanafi legal maxim: 'Whoever owns a thing owns its necessities and dependencies.' We see it applied to the mukatab, a slave in a kitabah contract. While his ownership is incomplete, he still owns his trade, and therefore he may spend small amounts necessary for that trade, such as minor gifts or charity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Maxim
Extract the maxim from the passage: 'مَنْ مَلَكَ شَيْئًا يَمْلِكُ مَا هُوَ مِنْ ضَرُورَاتِهِ وَتَوَابِعِهِ'. Understand its literal meaning: whoever owns a thing also owns what is necessary for it and its dependencies.
The maxim is a universal fiqh principle (qa'ida kulliya) used in many branches.
2
Understand the Context: The Mukatab
A mukatab is a slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying instalments. He has partial ownership of his earnings but is not fully free. His right of disposal is restricted.
The kitabah contract gives the slave a kind of ownership but with limitations.
3
Apply the Maxim to the Mukatab's Charity
The maxim is invoked to justify the permission for a small amount of charity. Since the mukatab owns his trade, he owns its necessities, which include small expenditures for hospitality or minor gifts.
The rule is: 'لا يتصدق إلا بالشيء اليسير' — he may not give charity except for a small thing.
4
Analyze the Proof via Qiyas
The proof is a qiyas (analogy) to sale: just as a sale contract inherently implies transfer of ownership without explicit mention, the kitabah contract inherently implies freedom upon payment. This supports the mukatab's limited ownership.
The phrase 'وَلَنَا أَنَّ مُوجِبَ الْعَقْدِ يَثْبُتُ مِنْ غَيْرِ تَصْرِيحٍ بِهِ' establishes this reasoning.
5
Recognize the Limit: Small vs. Large Gifts
The exception is only for 'small amount' (الشيء اليسير). Anything beyond that requires the master's permission. The small amount is defined as less than a dirham according to the commentaries.
This limitation protects the master's right to the mukatab's earnings pending payment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ مَلَكَ شَيْئًا يَمْلِكُ مَا هُوَ مِنْ ضَرُورَاتِهِ وَتَوَابِعِهِ.
وَلَنَا أَنَّ مُوجِبَ الْعَقْدِ يَثْبُتُ مِنْ غَيْرِ تَصْرِيحٍ بِهِ وَمُوجِبُهُ هَاهُنَا ضَمُّ حُرِّيَّةِ الْيَدِ الْحَاصِلِ فِى الْحَالِ إِلَى حُرِّيَّةِ الرَّقَبَةِ عِنْدَ أَدَاءِ الْبَدَلِ فَيَثْبُتُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُصَرِّحُ بِهِ كَمَا فِي الْبَيْعِ فَإِنَّهُ يَثْبُتُ الْمِلْكُ بِهِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُصَرِّحُ بِكَوْنِهِ مُوجِبَهُ .
وَيَجُوزُ أَيِ التَّزَوُّجُ بِإِذْنِ الْمَوْلَى، لِأَنَّ الْمِلْكَ لَهُ مَعْنَى الْمِلْكِ قَائِمٌ فِيهِ فَهُوَ كَالْحُرِّ فَلَا يَجُوزُ لَهُ إِلَّا بِإِذْنٍ وَلَا يَهِبُ وَلَا يَتَصَدَّقُ إِلَّا بِالشَّيْءِ الْيَسِيرِ.
وَلَا يَتَصَدَّقُ لِأَنَّهُ تَبَرُّعٌ وَهُوَ لَا يَمْلِكُهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ بِالشَّيْءِ الْيَسِيرِ لِأَنَّهُ مِنْ ضَرُورَةِ التِّجَارَةِ، وَمَنْ مَلَكَ شَيْئًا مَلَكَ مَا هُوَ مِنْ ضَرُورَاتِهِ وَتَوَابِعِهِ.
Whoever owns a thing owns what is of its necessities and dependencies.
And we have that the requirement of the contract is established without explicit mention, and its requirement here is the joining of the freedom of the hand currently obtained to the freedom of the neck upon payment of the substitute, so it is established even if not explicitly stated, as in sale, for ownership is established by it even if not explicitly stating that it is its requirement.
And it is permissible, i.e., marriage with the permission of the master, because ownership has the meaning of ownership established in him, so he is like a free person; thus it is not permissible for him except with permission, and he does not give a gift nor charity except for a small thing.
And he does not give charity because it is a gratuitous act and he does not own it, except if it is a small thing, because it is from the necessities of trade, and whoever owns a thing owns what is from its necessities and dependencies.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental Hanafi legal maxim: 'Whoever owns something owns its necessities and dependencies.' It is applied here to the case of a mukatab (a slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying instalments). The author explains that the mukatab cannot give gifts or charity because he does not fully own the property he earns; his ownership is restricted pending the book-keeping contract. However, he is permitted to give a small amount in charity because such trivial expenditure is considered a necessity of his trade, and since he owns the trade, he also owns its necessities. The proof from al-Inayah argues that the contract of kitabah implies freedom upon payment by its very nature, just as a sale implies transfer of ownership. The extracts from al-Binayah and al-Lubab further clarify that the mukatab may marry with the master's permission, but may not give gifts or charity except for a small amount (less than a dirham). This ruling is based on the principle that the mukatab's right of disposal is limited due to his incomplete ownership.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitābah) with his master to earn his freedom by paying a fixed amount.
كتابة (kitābah): A contract between a master and a slave whereby the slave is to pay a sum to the master in instalments in exchange for freedom.
ضرورة (ḍarūrah): Necessity; here it refers to what is essential for the mukatab's trade, such as small hospitality expenses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle discussed, why can a mukatab give a small amount in charity but not a large amount?
A) Because charity is always allowed irrespective of ownership.
B) Because a small amount is considered a necessity of trade, and the mukatab owns the trade.
C) Because the master has no control over the mukatab's earnings.
D) Because the mukatab is fully free after signing the kitabah contract.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Whoever owns a thing owns its necessities' allows a mukatab to spend a small amount on charity as a trade necessity, but larger expenditures require the master's permission.

The Maxim of Choosing the Lesser Harm: Al-Darar al-Ashad Yuzal bi al-Darar al-Ahaqq
Introducing a maxim for weighing harms in the context of hajr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'al-darar al-ashad yuzal bi al-darar al-ahaqq' and apply it to cases of hajr (legal interdiction) for fiscal irresponsibility.

Core Idea
When two harms conflict, choose the lesser one.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi legal maxim: the more severe harm is removed by the less severe harm. Used widely across fiqh, this principle is particularly relevant in the chapter of hajr (legal interdiction), where a fiscally irresponsible person may be placed under guardianship. While interdiction restricts personal freedom, that harm is tolerated because it prevents the greater harm of total financial ruin. Understanding this maxim helps you weigh competing harms in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Deconstruct the Maxim
Break down the Arabic phrase: الضرر الأشد يزال بالضرر الأحق. Identify each word: الضرر (harm), الأشد (more severe), يزال (is removed), بالضرر (by the harm), الأحق (less severe). Understand the grammatical structure: subject-verb-prepositional phrase.
The term الأحق literally means 'more deserving' but here it is used as a synonym for الأخف (lighter).
2
Understand the Key Terms
Define 'ḍarar' (harm), 'ashadd' (more severe), and 'ahaqq' (less severe). Recognize that these are comparative adjectives ranking degrees of harm.
In Hanafi usul, harms are weighed objectively, not subjectively.
3
Identify the Scope of Application
The maxim applies when two harms are inevitable and one must be chosen. The greater harm is removed by tolerating the lesser harm. This is not a blank check to inflict harm; the inflicted harm must be demonstrably less severe.
This is related to the principle of 'ahwan al-baliyatain' (the lesser of two evils).
4
Apply to Hajr
In the context of hajr, a spendthrift (safih) who is wasteful may be placed under guardianship. The harm of restricted freedom is less severe than the harm of complete financial loss. Therefore, the maxim justifies the interdiction.
This application appears in al-Hidayah and other Hanafi works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اَلضَّرَرُ الأَشَدُّ يُزَالُ بِالضَّرَرِ الأَحَقِ.
The more severe harm is removed by the less severe harm.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a well-known Hanafi legal maxim: 'The greater harm is removed by the lesser harm.' It is applied in the context of hajr (legal interdiction) for a person who is fiscally irresponsible. The author explains that when there is harm in both permitting and prohibiting, the more severe harm is eliminated by tolerating the less severe one. For example, placing a spendthrift under guardianship causes some harm (restriction of freedom), but this is lesser compared to the harm of his financial ruin. This maxim is also known as 'ahwan al-baliyatain' (the lesser of the two evils). In this section of al-Hidayah, the principle is used to justify the hajr (interdiction) over a free, adult, and sane person who is wasteful, to protect his wealth. The commentary stresses that the harm inflicted must be lighter than the harm prevented.

Key Terms
ضرر (ḍarar): Harm or injury. In this maxim, it refers to any detrimental consequence, whether physical or financial.
أشد (ashadd): More severe; the greater degree of harm.
أحق (aḥaqq): Less severe; literally 'more deserving' but here means 'lesser' in degree of harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi maxim 'al-darar al-ashad yuzal bi al-darar al-ahaqq,' what condition must be met when applying it?
A) The harm inflicted must be equal to the harm removed.
B) The harm inflicted must be less severe than the harm removed.
C) The harm inflicted must be greater than the harm removed.
D) Any harm can be inflicted regardless of severity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-darar al-ashad yuzal bi al-darar al-ahaqq' mandates that when two harms are unavoidable, the lesser harm is tolerated to prevent the greater harm.

The Greater Harm is Removed by the Lesser: Interdiction of the Free Adult Safīh
States a legal maxim and applies it to interdiction of a free adult safīh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ḍarar al-ashaddu yuzālu bi al-ḍarar al-aḥaqq' to justify the Hanafi ruling that a free adult safīh is not interdicted.

Core Idea
Interdiction destroys human dignity – a greater harm – so squandering is tolerated as the lesser harm.

We begin with the chapter on interdiction. The author introduces a foundational maxim: 'The greater harm is removed by the lesser harm.' This principle guides the Hanafi position on the financial capacity of a free adult safīh. Understanding this maxim is key to grasping the school's balancing of harms.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its meaning
State the Arabic maxim and explain that when two harms conflict, the greater must be averted even if it requires enduring the lesser.
2
Identify the two conflicting harms in the case
Harm A: Stripping the safīh of legal capacity (interdiction) – destroys his human dignity. Harm B: His squandering (tabdhīr) – financial waste.
The safīh is a free adult, not a slave or minor.
3
Apply the maxim to weigh the harms
Interdiction is the greater harm because it reduces a human to an animal. Squandering is lesser. Therefore, the greater harm (interdiction) must be avoided even if it means enduring the lesser harm (squandering).
4
Derive the ruling
The free adult safīh is not interdicted; his transactions are permissible (jāʾiz) according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
The Sahibayn may have a different view, but the relied-upon position is the Imam's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحجر
باب الحجر للفساد
(۱۳۲) اَلضَّرَرُ الأَشَدُّ يُزَالُ بِالضَّرَرِ الأَحَقِ.
ترجمہ: ضر راشد کو زائل کیا جائے گا ضر را خف کے ساتھ۔

Book of Interdiction. Chapter: Interdiction for Corruption. (132) The greater harm is removed by the lesser harm. Translation: The greater harm will be removed by the lesser harm. 184 Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned maxim is that where there is harm on both the positive and negative sides, it will be seen which harm is greater and higher; to remove the greater harm, the lighter and lesser harm will be endured. This is called by jurists 'the lesser of two evils'. The author of Al-Hidayah, after mentioning this maxim, intends that a free, sane, adult, and non-interdicted individual will not have restrictions placed upon him; rather, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, his financial transactions are permissible, because placing restrictions on him and stripping him of his importance destroys his humanity and puts him in the rank of animals, and this is a greater harm, while squandering (extravagance) is a lesser harm, and a greater harm is not endured to avert a lesser harm; rather, the opposite holds. Therefore, a free, sane, adult safīh will not be interdicted to avert squandering, and his transactions will be considered permissible despite squandering. (And it is permissible) i.e., marriage with the permission of the master, because ownership (has the meaning of ownership established in him, so he is like a free person, so it is not permissible for him except with permission, and he may not gift or give charity except for a trivial thing) i.e., less than a dirham, because it is a small amount that people overlook, said by the author of Al-Shari'a (because gifting and charity are voluntary, and he is not a full owner to transfer ownership) (except that a trivial thing is from the necessities of trade, because he cannot avoid hospitality). (Al-Binaya: Kitab al-Mukatib, chapter on what is permissible for a mukatib to do, vol. 10, p. 389) (And he does not give charity) because it is voluntary and he does not own it (except) if it is (a trivial thing) because it is a necessity of trade, and whoever owns something owns its necessities and accessories. (Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab: Kitab al-Mukatib, vol. 3, p. 128) Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Hajr, Bab al-Hajr li al-Fasad, vol. 3, p. 355. Usool-e-Hidayah, Third volume, p. 185. Restriction will not be imposed on a person; rather, his financial transactions are permissible according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because if restriction is imposed and his importance is stripped, this destroys his humanity and places him among animals, and this is a greater harm, while squandering (extravagance) is a lesser harm. A lesser harm is not averted by bearing a greater harm; rather, the opposite. Hence, a free, sane, adult safīh will not be interdicted to avert squandering, and his transactions will be considered permissible despite squandering. (133) Foolishness does not nullify the rights of people. Translation: Foolishness cannot nullify the rights of people. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned maxim is that the rights of people will not lapse from a safīh, a foolish, ignorant person due to his foolishness; rather, the payment of people's rights is obligatory upon him. The author of Al-Hidayah, after mentioning this maxim, states that zakat will be paid from the wealth of the safīh, i.e., the ignorant person, and similarly the maintenance of his wife and children, and the maintenance of those whom he is obligated to support, all will be given from the wealth of this safīh, because zakat is among the rights of the Shariah. (And this) i.e., the absence of interdiction (because) in interdiction, stripping his legal capacity involves the destruction of his humanity, and this is apparent. (Al-Inaya: Kitab al-Hajr, Bab al-Hajr li al-Fasad, vol. 9, p. 261) And this) i.e., the absence of interdiction (because in stripping his legal capacity is the destruction of his humanity and his being placed with animals) considering his lack of control in transactions (and it) i.e., interdiction upon him is a greater harm than squandering, so the greater harm (interdiction) is not endured to repel the lesser harm (squandering). (Al-Binaya: Kitab al-Hajr, Bab al-Hajr li al-Fasad, vol. 10, p. 90) Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Hajr, Bab al-Hajr li al-Fasad, vol. 3, p. 357.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage from Usool-e-Hidayah presents two important legal maxims (qawāʿid fiqhiyya) and their application to the topic of al-ḥajr li al-fasād (interdiction due to fiscal corruption).

First Maxim (132): The greater harm is removed by the lesser harm (al-ḍarar al-ashaddu yuzālu bi al-ḍarar al-aḥaqq). The commentary explains that when both alternatives involve harm, the lesser harm is endured to avoid the greater harm. This is known as 'ahwan al-balīyatayn' (the lesser of two evils). The author of Al-Hidayah applies this to the case of a free, sane, adult safīh (squanderer). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, such a person should not be interdicted, because placing him under financial restriction strips him of his human dignity and reduces him to the level of animals – this is the greater harm. His squandering (tabdhīr) is a lesser harm. Since the greater harm cannot be endured to avert the lesser, the safīh remains unrestricted and his transactions are valid.

Second Maxim (133): Foolishness does not nullify the rights of people (al-safahu lā yubṭilu ḥuqūq al-nās). This means that the rights of others (ḥuqūq al-nās) are not abrogated by a person's foolishness. The passage applies this to the safīh: he is still obligated to pay zakāt and provide maintenance (nafaqah) for his wife, children, and other dependents from his wealth. These rights are not suspended because of his irresponsibility, as zakāt is among the rights of the Sharīʿah and nafaqah is a right of others. Both obligations remain binding on the safīh, and his wealth is liable for them.

The passage cites several Hanafi authorities: Al-Hidayah, Al-Bināyah, Al-Lubāb, and Al-ʿInāyah, all of which support these rulings.

Key Terms
الضرر الأشد (al-ḍarar al-ashadd): The greater or more severe harm; that which is more damaging to a person's dignity or welfare.
الضرر الأحق (al-ḍarar al-aḥaqq): The lesser or lighter harm; that which is more appropriate to endure to avoid the greater harm.
السفه (al-safah): Foolishness, reckless spending, fiscal irresponsibility. The quality of a safīh.
حقوق الناس (ḥuqūq al-nās): Rights owed to other people, such as financial obligations (zakāt, nafaqah) that are due from a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-ḍarar al-ashaddu yuzālu bi al-ḍarar al-aḥaqq' governs the Hanafi position that a free adult safīh is not interdicted despite his squandering.

Foolishness Does Not Nullify the Rights of People: Obligations of the Safīh
States a second maxim and applies it to the safīh's obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a free adult safīh remains obligated to pay zakāt and nafaqah despite his foolishness.

Core Idea
Foolishness does not discharge the rights of others; the safīh's wealth is still liable for zakāt and maintenance.

The second maxim clarifies that while the safīh is not interdicted, his financial irresponsibility does not exempt him from core obligations. Rights of Allah (zakāt) and rights of people (nafaqah) remain mandatory. This ensures that his folly does not harm others.

Guided Path
1
State the second maxim
State the maxim: 'al-safahu lā yubṭilu ḥuqūq al-nās' (Foolishness does not nullify the rights of people).
2
Identify the rights that remain
Zakāt (right of Allah) and nafaqah for wife, children, and dependents (rights of people) are not waived.
These are obligatory duties established by Sharīʿah.
3
Understand why they are not nullified
Because these rights are not contingent on the person's prudence; they are based on the wealth itself and the relationship.
4
Apply to the safīh
The judge must extract zakāt from his wealth and enforce nafaqah payment, even if the safīh objects.

Book of Interdiction. Chapter: Interdiction for Corruption. (132) The greater harm is removed by the lesser harm. Translation: The greater harm will be removed by the lesser harm. 184 Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned maxim is that where there is harm on both the positive and negative sides, it will be seen which harm is greater and higher; to remove the greater harm, the lighter and lesser harm will be endured. This is called by jurists 'the lesser of two evils'. The author of Al-Hidayah, after mentioning this maxim, intends that a free, sane, adult, and non-interdicted individual will not have restrictions placed upon him; rather, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, his financial transactions are permissible, because placing restrictions on him and stripping him of his importance destroys his humanity and puts him in the rank of animals, and this is a greater harm, while squandering (extravagance) is a lesser harm, and a greater harm is not endured to avert a lesser harm; rather, the opposite holds. Therefore, a free, sane, adult safīh will not be interdicted to avert squandering, and his transactions will be considered permissible despite squandering. (And it is permissible) i.e., marriage with the permission of the master, because ownership (has the meaning of ownership established in him, so he is like a free person, so it is not permissible for him except with permission, and he may not gift or give charity except for a trivial thing) i.e., less than a dirham, because it is a small amount that people overlook, said by the author of Al-Shari'a (because gifting and charity are voluntary, and he is not a full owner to transfer ownership) (except that a trivial thing is from the necessities of trade, because he cannot avoid hospitality). (Al-Binaya: Kitab al-Mukatib, chapter on what is permissible for a mukatib to do, vol. 10, p. 389) (And he does not give charity) because it is voluntary and he does not own it (except) if it is (a trivial thing) because it is a necessity of trade, and whoever owns something owns its necessities and accessories. (Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab: Kitab al-Mukatib, vol. 3, p. 128) Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Hajr, Bab al-Hajr li al-Fasad, vol. 3, p. 355. Usool-e-Hidayah, Third volume, p. 185. Restriction will not be imposed on a person; rather, his financial transactions are permissible according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because if restriction is imposed and his importance is stripped, this destroys his humanity and places him among animals, and this is a greater harm, while squandering (extravagance) is a lesser harm. A lesser harm is not averted by bearing a greater harm; rather, the opposite. Hence, a free, sane, adult safīh will not be interdicted to avert squandering, and his transactions will be considered permissible despite squandering. (133) Foolishness does not nullify the rights of people. Translation: Foolishness cannot nullify the rights of people. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned maxim is that the rights of people will not lapse from a safīh, a foolish, ignorant person due to his foolishness; rather, the payment of people's rights is obligatory upon him. The author of Al-Hidayah, after mentioning this maxim, states that zakat will be paid from the wealth of the safīh, i.e., the ignorant person, and similarly the maintenance of his wife and children, and the maintenance of those whom he is obligated to support, all will be given from the wealth of this safīh, because zakat is among the rights of the Shariah. (And this) i.e., the absence of interdiction (because) in interdiction, stripping his legal capacity involves the destruction of his humanity, and this is apparent. (Al-Inaya: Kitab al-Hajr, Bab al-Hajr li al-Fasad, vol. 9, p. 261) And this) i.e., the absence of interdiction (because in stripping his legal capacity is the destruction of his humanity and his being placed with animals) considering his lack of control in transactions (and it) i.e., interdiction upon him is a greater harm than squandering, so the greater harm (interdiction) is not endured to repel the lesser harm (squandering). (Al-Binaya: Kitab al-Hajr, Bab al-Hajr li al-Fasad, vol. 10, p. 90) Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Hajr, Bab al-Hajr li al-Fasad, vol. 3, p. 357.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage from Usool-e-Hidayah presents two important legal maxims (qawāʿid fiqhiyya) and their application to the topic of al-ḥajr li al-fasād (interdiction due to fiscal corruption).

First Maxim (132): The greater harm is removed by the lesser harm (al-ḍarar al-ashaddu yuzālu bi al-ḍarar al-aḥaqq). The commentary explains that when both alternatives involve harm, the lesser harm is endured to avoid the greater harm. This is known as 'ahwan al-balīyatayn' (the lesser of two evils). The author of Al-Hidayah applies this to the case of a free, sane, adult safīh (squanderer). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, such a person should not be interdicted, because placing him under financial restriction strips him of his human dignity and reduces him to the level of animals – this is the greater harm. His squandering (tabdhīr) is a lesser harm. Since the greater harm cannot be endured to avert the lesser, the safīh remains unrestricted and his transactions are valid.

Second Maxim (133): Foolishness does not nullify the rights of people (al-safahu lā yubṭilu ḥuqūq al-nās). This means that the rights of others (ḥuqūq al-nās) are not abrogated by a person's foolishness. The passage applies this to the safīh: he is still obligated to pay zakāt and provide maintenance (nafaqah) for his wife, children, and other dependents from his wealth. These rights are not suspended because of his irresponsibility, as zakāt is among the rights of the Sharīʿah and nafaqah is a right of others. Both obligations remain binding on the safīh, and his wealth is liable for them.

The passage cites several Hanafi authorities: Al-Hidayah, Al-Bināyah, Al-Lubāb, and Al-ʿInāyah, all of which support these rulings.

Key Terms
الضرر الأشد (al-ḍarar al-ashadd): The greater or more severe harm; that which is more damaging to a person's dignity or welfare.
الضرر الأحق (al-ḍarar al-aḥaqq): The lesser or lighter harm; that which is more appropriate to endure to avoid the greater harm.
السفه (al-safah): Foolishness, reckless spending, fiscal irresponsibility. The quality of a safīh.
حقوق الناس (ḥuqūq al-nās): Rights owed to other people, such as financial obligations (zakāt, nafaqah) that are due from a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is exempt from all financial obligations
B) He must still pay zakāt and provide nafaqah
C) He can be interdicted to force payment
D) His wife must pay for her own maintenance

Key Takeaway
Foolishness does not nullify financial obligations; the safīh must still pay zakāt and provide nafaqah.

Ghasb of Immovable Property and Liability for Natural Calamity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for destroyed immovable property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on liability for immovable property destroyed by natural calamity and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf versus Muhammad.

Core Idea
For ghasb of immovables, liability for natural destruction is negated because the owner's possession is not directly removed.

When someone usurps movable property, they are liable for its destruction. But what if the property is land or a building, and it is destroyed by a natural calamity like a flood? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on this. This lesson explores that dispute, focusing on the definition of ghasb and how it applies to immovable property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is whether a usurper of immovable property is liable for its destruction when the destruction occurs through a natural calamity (āfa samāwiyya).
The act of ghasb itself is haram; the dispute is about liability for subsequent destruction.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the usurper is not liable. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that he is liable.
These are the three major Hanafi imams; the majority is with the two shaykhs.
3
Analyze the majority proof
Ghasb is defined as establishing possession by removing the owner's hand through an act on the property. In immovables, the owner's hand is only removed by evicting him—an act on the owner, not on the property. Therefore, ghasb is not fully realized, negating liability.
Key wording: 'act on the owner' versus 'act on the property.'
4
Understand Muhammad's proof
Muhammad argues that establishing the hand necessarily removes the owner's hand, as two hands cannot coexist. Thus liability applies.
This is a more general interpretation of ghasb.
5
Identify the muftā bihī
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (no liability), as confirmed in al-Tashīḥ.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمہ: غصب عین میں فعل کے ذریعے مالک کے قبضے کو زائل کر کے قبضہ ثابت کرنے کا نام ہے۔ تشریح مذکور و اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ شیخین رحمہما اللہ کے نزدیک غصب کا معنی ہے کہ غاصب شے مغصوبہ میں فعل غصب کے ذریعے مالک کے قبضے کو زائل کر کے اپنا قبضہ ثابت کرے، اور یہ صرف منقولی چیزوں میں ہو سکتا ہے، غیر منقولی چیز میں یہ معنی متفق نہیں ہوسکتا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر غاصب نے زمین غصب کی اور وہ زمین کسی آفت سماوی سے ہلاک ہوگئی، تو غاصب شیخین رحمہما اللہ کے نزدیک ضامن نہیں ہوگا، کیونکہ غصب کا معنی ہے مغصو بہ شے میں فعل غصب سرانجام دے کر مالک کے قبضے کو زائل کرنا اور غصب کی یہ تعریف عقار پر صادق نہیں آتی ، کیونکہ عقار سے اسی وقت مالک کا قبضہ ختم ہوگا جب مالک کو عقار سے بے دخل کر دیا جائے اور یہ بے دخلی مالک کی ذات میں تصرف ہوگا نہ کہ زمین میں اور جب مغصوبہ زمین میں غصب مؤثر نہیں ہوگا تو غصب محقق بھی نہیں ہوگا اور جب غصب متحقق نہیں ہوگا تو اس زمین کی ہلاکت سے غاصب پر ضمان نہیں ہوگا۔
(فَإِذَا غَصَبَ عَقَارًا فَهَلَكَ فِي يَدِهِ بِغَيْرِ صُنْعِهِ لَمْ يَضْمَنْهُ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِي يُوسُفَ رَحِمَهُمَا اللَّهُ. وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ يَضْمَنُهُ.... وَلَابِي حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِي يُوسُفَ أَنَّ الْغَصْبَ إِثْبَاتُ الْيَدِ بِإِزَالَةِ يَدِ الْمَالِكِ) أَيْ بِسَبَبٍ ذَلِكَ (وَهَذَا) أَيْ هَذَا الْمَجُمُوعُ وَلَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقَارِ، لِأَنَّ يَدَ الْمَالِكِ لَا تَزُولُ إِلَّا بِإِخْرَاجِهِ) أَى بِإِخْرَاجِ الْمَالِكِ عَنْهَا) أَيْ عَنْ الْعَقَارِ بِمَعْنَى الضَّيْعَةِ أَوْ الدَّارِ (وَهُوَ أَى الْإِخْرَاجُ فِعْلٌ فِي الْمَالِكِ لَا فِي الْعَقَارِ) فَانْتَفَى إِزَالَةُ الْيَدِ وَالْكُلُّ يَنْتَفِي بِانْتِفَاءِ جُزْئِهِ. (العناية: كتاب الغصب، ج ۹ ص ۳۲۳)
Translation: Ghasb is the establishment of possession by removing the owner's possession through an act. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the meaning of ghasb is that the usurper removes the owner's possession from the usurped object through an act of usurpation and establishes his own possession. This can only occur in movable property; it cannot be conceived in immovable property. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, states that if a usurper usurps land and that land is destroyed by a heavenly calamity, then according to the two shaykhs, the usurper is not liable, because the meaning of ghasb is to remove the owner's possession by performing an act of usurpation in the usurped object, and this definition does not apply to immovable property, because the owner's possession of immovable property ends only when he is evicted from the property, and this eviction is an act upon the owner, not upon the land. When usurpation is not effective upon usurped land, then ghasb is not realized, and when ghasb is not realized, there is no liability upon the usurper for the destruction of that land. (If he usurps immovable property and it is destroyed in his hand without his act, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say he is not liable. Muhammad says he is liable. And for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, ghasb is the establishment of the hand by removing the owner's hand, i.e., by that cause. This totality is not conceivable in immovable property, because the owner's hand does not cease except by his removal, i.e., by removing the owner from it, i.e., from the property, meaning the estate or house. This removal is an act upon the owner, not upon the property. Thus removal of the hand is negated, and the whole is negated by the negation of its part.) (Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Ghasb, vol. 9 p. 323)
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability for usurped immovable property destroyed by a natural calamity. The author first defines ghasb as establishing possession by removing the owner's possession through an act, which can only occur with movable property. He then explains that if a usurper takes land and it is destroyed by a heavenly calamity (like a flood), Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the usurper is not liable, while Muhammad holds him liable. The reasoning is that ghasb of immovables does not fully remove the owner's hand, because the owner's possession only ends by physically evicting him—an act on the owner, not on the property. Since the definition of ghasb requires removal of the owner's hand, and this is not achieved in immovables, ghasb is not fully realized, and thus liability does not attach. The relied-upon position (al-mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as stated in al-Tashih.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Taking possession of another's property by force, removing the owner's possession through an act.
عقار (ʿiqār): Immovable property such as land or buildings.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what is the reason a usurper of immovable property is not liable for destruction by a natural calamity?
A) Because the property is too large to be considered usurped
B) Because the owner's hand is only removed by an act on the owner, not on the property
C) Because natural calamities are beyond human control
D) Because the usurper did not intend to destroy the property

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a usurper is not liable for the destruction of immovable property by a natural calamity, because ghasb of immovables does not fully remove the owner's possession.

Profit from Usurped or Entrusted Property is Not Permissible
Ruling that profit from unauthorized use is impermissible and must be given in charity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that profit from unauthorized use of usurped or entrusted property is impermissible and must be given in charity.

Core Idea
Profit from unauthorized use of another's property is impure; the usurper must give it all in charity.

This lesson examines a clear Hanafi ruling that when a usurper or bailee uses the property without permission and earns profit, that profit is not permissible for them. The ruling is agreed upon by Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. We will explore the rationale and the practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Recognize that the act is trading with usurped or entrusted property without the owner's permission.
2
Identify the ruling
The profit (ribḥ) is not permissible (la yatibu) – meaning it is haram for the one who misused the property to keep.
3
Understand the basis
The profit is impure because it was earned by transacting in another's property without authorization.
4
Note the consequence
The usurper or bailee must give the entire profit in charity; they cannot keep any part of it.
5
Recognize the consensus
This ruling is agreed upon by Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (al-ṭarafayn), so it is a strong position within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اللباب: كتاب الغصب، ج ۲ ص ۱۸۹)
الهداية كتاب الغصب، ج ۳ ص ۳۷۵
(136) The usurper and the bailee, when they transact with the usurped or entrusted property and earn profit, the profit is not permissible for them according to both parties. Translation: When a usurper transacts with the usurped object and a bailee transacts with the entrusted object and earns profit, then this profit is not permissible for them according to both (imams). Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that if the usurper or the bailee transacted with the usurped or entrusted property, i.e., used it in trade and earned profit, then this profit is not permissible for them; rather, they should give all the profit in charity, because they earned profit by transacting in another's property without permission, thus it became impure. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, proves this point: if a usurper usurped a thousand rupees, then bought a slave girl with it, then sold her for two thousand, then bought another slave girl for two thousand and sold her for three thousand, then the usurper must give the profit of two thousand in charity according to both (imams), because the usurper earned profit by transacting with the usurped property. And ghasb is only realized in what is movable and transferable, because ghasb is only realized in that, not in others, because removal of the hand is by transfer. And if he usurps immovable property and it is destroyed in his hand by a heavenly calamity like an overpowering flood, he is not liable according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, due to the non-realization of ghasb by removal of the hand, because immovable property is in its place without transfer, and keeping the owner away from it is an act on the owner, not on the property, so it is as if the owner is kept away from livestock. Muhammad said: He is liable due to the realization of establishing the hand, and its necessary consequence is the removal of the owner's hand, because it is impossible for two hands to be on one place at one time. Al-Tashih said: The correct is their statement. (Al-Lubab: Kitab al-Ghasb, vol. 2 p. 189) (Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Ghasb, vol. 3 p. 375)
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage states a ruling that any profit earned by a usurper or a bailee through unauthorized use of the property is not permissible for them; they must give the entire profit in charity. The Urdu commentary explains that this is because they transacted in another's property without permission, rendering the profit impure. An example is given of a usurper who uses usurped money to trade in slave girls and makes a profit; that profit must be given in charity. The passage then includes an Arabic quote reiterating the earlier dispute about immovable property, likely as a cross-reference, but the primary ruling here is the impermissibility of profit from unauthorized use. This ruling is agreed upon by both sides (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) — 'inda al-tarafayn.

Key Terms
ربح (ribḥ): Profit earned from trade or investment.
مودع (mūdiʿ): A person who has been entrusted with property as a deposit (wadi'ah).
طرفین (ṭarafayn): Both parties; likely referring to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (or Abu Yusuf) — indicating consensus within the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Keep it as his own
B) Return the principal but keep the profit
C) Give the entire profit in charity
D) Give half the profit to the owner

Key Takeaway
Profit from unauthorized use of another's property is impure; the entire profit must be given in charity.

Profit from Usurped or Deposited Property: Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
States ruling that profit from usurped or deposited property is impermissible and must be given in charity, with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on profit earned from misappropriated property, explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and identify the point of disagreement with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Profit earned by using another's property without ownership is impure and must be given in charity.

The passage presents a classic Hanafi ruling on the fate of profit when a usurper or depositary uses another's property in a profitable transaction. The general principle is that such profit is not permissible for the wrongdoer because the disposal was not based on ownership. However, there is an intra-madhhab difference: Imam Abu Yusuf holds a different view, which is referenced but not fully elaborated here. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of the majority position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The passage refers to a usurper (ghasib) and a depositary (mūdi‘) who dispose of property that is not their own—usurped property or a deposit—and earn a profit.
The depositary is the one entrusted with the deposit; note that the text uses 'mūdi‘' for the depositary, though technically it means the depositor. This usage is noted in some Hanafi texts.
2
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
According to them, the profit is not permissible (la yatibu) and must be given in charity. The profit is considered impure (khabīth).
3
Understand the reasoning
The disposal was in non-ownership because the cause of price (sabab al-thaman) was absent at the time of disposal. Therefore, ownership cannot relate back to before the transaction, making the profit impure.
This is a rational proof (dalīl ‘aqlī).
4
Identify the intra-madhhab dispute
Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees (khilāfan li Abī Yūsuf). The text notes that the answer of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is more apparent, especially in the case of deposit.
Abu Yusuf’s exact position is not given here, but it is implied that he considers the profit permissible or owned.
5
Apply the ruling to the example
A usurper takes 1000 dirhams, buys a slave girl, sells for 2000, buys another, sells for 3000. Total profit 2000 dirhams. All profit must be given in charity.
This example is from al-Jāmi‘ al-Ṣaghīr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْغَاصِبُ وَالْمُوْدَعُ إِذَا تَصَرَّفَ فِي الْمَغْصُوبِ أَوِ الْوَدِيْعَةِ وَرَبِحَ لَا يَطِيبُ لَهُ الرِّبْحُ عِنْدَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ. وَمَنْ غَصَبَ أَلْفًا فَاشْتَرَى بِهَا جَارِيَةً الْغَاصِبُ إِذَا تَصَرَّفَ فِي الْمَغْصُوبِ أَوْ الْمُودِعُ فِى الْوَدِيعَةِ وَرَبِحَ فِيهِ لَا يَطِيبُ لَهُ الرِّبْحُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ رَحِمَهُمَا اللَّهُ، خِلافًا لأبى يُوسُفَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ وَقَدْ مَرَّ فِي الدَّلَائِلِ. وَجَوَابُهُمَا فِي الْوَدِيعَةِ أَظْهَرُ لِمَا ذَكَرُنَا أَنَّهُ لَا يَسْتَنِدُ الْمِلْكُ إِلَى مَا قَبْلَ التَّصَرُّفِ لِانْعِدَامِ سَبَبِ الثَّمَنِ، فَكَانَ التَّصَرُّفَ فِي غَيْرِ مِلْكِهِ مُطْلَقًا فَيَكُونُ الرِّبْحُ خَبِيئًا. قال: ومن غصب ألفا أى قال في الجامع الصغير: فاشترى بها جارية فباعها بألفين ثم اشترى بالألفين جارية فباعها بثلاثة آلاف درهم، فإنه يتصدق بجميع الربح، وهذا عندهما أى التصدق بجميع الربح عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله لأنه ملك خبيث وبه قال الشافعي في الجديد، وأحمد في رواية
The usurper and the depositary, when they dispose of the usurped property or the deposit and make a profit, the profit is not permissible for them according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). And whoever usurps a thousand and buys a slave girl with it... (the same ruling) ... profit is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Their answer regarding the deposit is more apparent because the ownership does not relate back to before the disposal due to the absence of the reason for the price, so the disposal was absolutely in non-ownership, thus the profit is impure. He said: And whoever usurps a thousand, meaning he said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'he buys a slave girl with it, sells her for two thousand, then buys a slave girl with the two thousand and sells her for three thousand dirhams, then he gives all the profit in charity, and this according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that all the profit is given in charity because it is impure property, and al-Shafi'i said the same in his new opinion, and Ahmad in one narration.'
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on profit derived from unauthorized use of another's property, specifically by a usurper (ghasib) or a depositary (mudi'). The ruling states that such profit is not permissible (la yatibu) for them and must be given in charity. The text then provides a concrete example: a person usurps one thousand dirhams, uses it to buy a slave girl, sells her for two thousand, then uses that money to buy another slave girl and sells her for three thousand. The total profit of two thousand must be given in charity. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. Imam Abu Yusuf differs, and the text notes that his objection was addressed in the earlier discussion of evidences. The reasoning for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's position is that the ownership of the profit cannot be attributed to a time before the transaction because the usurper did not have a valid cause of ownership (sabab al-thaman) at that time. Hence, the disposal was entirely in non-ownership, and the profit is considered 'khābith' (impure) property. The passage also cites that Imam al-Shafi'i in his new opinion and Imam Ahmad in one narration agree with this view.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghasib): One who takes another's property without right and without permission.
مغصوب (maghsūb): The property that has been usurped.
مودع (mūdi‘): A person with whom something is deposited (depositary).
ودیعة (wadī‘ah): Property left with someone for safekeeping without compensation.
ربح (ribḥ): Profit or gain from a commercial transaction.
خبیث (khabīth): Impure or unlawful property that is not permissible to keep; must be given in charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Keep it as his own property.
B) Return it to the original owner.
C) Give it all in charity.
D) Split it with the original owner.

Key Takeaway
Profit earned by using another's property without authorization is not permissible and must be given in charity.

Profit from Usurped or Deposited Property: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on profit from usurped/deposited property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit from usurped or deposited property, identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement, and apply the mufta bihi position in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Profit from usurped or deposited property is khabīth and must be given in charity; Abu Yusuf's contrary view is weaker.

When someone wrongfully uses another's property (usurpation or deposit) and trades with it, the profit earned is not automatically permissible. This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the profit is filthy and must be given in charity, while Abu Yusuf considers it lawful. We will analyse the reasoning and the preferred position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
Recognise that the discussion covers two scenarios: a usurper (ghāṣib) or a depositor (mūdi') who disposes of the property (maghsūb or wadī'ah) and earns profit.
The depositor here is the person entrusted with the deposit, not the owner.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the profit is not halal (lā yaṭību lahu al-ribḥ) and must be given in charity; it is considered khabīth (filthy).
This is the position in the ẓāhir al-riwāyah.
3
Note the dissenting view
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the profit becomes halal for the wrongdoer. The text does not provide his full proof, but the Hanafi response is that ownership cannot retroact because there was no valid cause of ownership at the time of disposition.
The dissenting view is considered weak, especially for deposits.
4
Examine the reasoning for the majority view
The key principle: ownership does not relate back to before the disposition because the cause of ownership (sabab al-thaman) was absent. Hence the trade was entirely in non-ownership, making the profit khabīth.
This is a rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text in Al-Hidayah and Al-Inayah states that the answer of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding deposit is more apparent (aẓhar). Thus the fatwa is to give the entire profit in charity.
Shafi'i (new view) and Ahmad (one narration) agree with this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور جب غاصب مخصو بہ شے میں تصرف کر کے نفع کمائے وہ اس کے لئے حلال نہیں ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی دو ہزار جو نفع ہے وہ اس کے لئے حلال نہیں ، اسی طرح ودیعت کی چیزوں سے کمایا جانے والا نفع بھی حلال نہیں ۔ غاصب چونکہ ان روپوں کا مالک نہیں ہے اس لیے یہ غیر کی ملک میں تصرف کرنا ہے، اور غیر کی ملک میں غاصبانہ تصرف کر کے نفع کمانا خبیث ہے، اس لیے یہ اس کے حلال نہیں ہے۔

(وَمَنْ غَصَبَ أَلْفًا فَاشْتَرَى بِهَا جَارِيَةً الْغَاصِبُ إِذَا تَصَرَّفَ فِي الْمَغْضُوبِ أَوْ الْمُودِعُ فِى الْوَدِيعَةِ وَرَبِحَ فِيهِ لَا يَطِيبُ لَهُ الرِّبْحُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ رَحِمَهُمَا اللَّهُ، خِلافًا لأبى يُوسُفَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ وَقَدْ مَرَّ فِي الدَّلَائِلِ. وَجَوَابُهُمَا فِي الْوَدِيعَةِ أَظْهَرُ لِمَا ذَكَرُنَا أَنَّهُ لَا يَسْتَنِدُ الْمِلْكُ إِلَى مَا قَبْلَ التَّصَرُّفِ لِانْعِدَامِ سَبَبِ الشَّمَانِ، فَكَانَ التَّصَرُّفَ فِي غَيْرِ مِلْكِهِ مُطْلَقًا فَيَكُونُ الرِّبْحُ خَبِيئًا. (العناية: كتاب الغصب، ج ۹ ص ۳۳۰)
قال: ومن غصب ألفا أى قال في الجامع الصغير" (فاشترى بها جارية فباعها بألفين ثم اشترى بالألفين جارية فباعها بثلاثة آلاف درهم، فإنه يتصدق بجميع الربح، وهذا عندهما أى التصدق بجميع الربح عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله لأنه ملك خبيث وبه قال الشافعي في الجديد، وأحمد في رواية (البناية: كتاب الغصب، ج ۱۱ ص ۲۰۰
And when the usurper disposes of the usurped item and earns profit, it is not permissible for him. Thus in the mentioned case, the profit of two thousand is not permissible for him. Similarly, profit earned from deposited items is not permissible. Because the usurper is not the owner of those rupees, so this is disposing in another's property, and earning profit by usurpation in another's property is filthy, so it is not permissible for him.
(And whoever usurps one thousand and buys a slave-girl with it, the usurper when he disposes of the usurped item or the depositor in the deposit and profits thereby, the profit is not lawful for him according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, contrary to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, and it has passed in the proofs. And their answer regarding the deposit is more apparent because as we mentioned, ownership does not relate back to before the disposition due to the absence of the cause of price, so the disposition was entirely in other than his ownership, thus the profit is filthy. (Al-Inayah: Book of Ghasp, vol. 9 p. 330)
He said: And whoever usurps one thousand - he said in Al-Jami' al-Saghir: then buys a slave-girl with it, then sells it for two thousand, then buys a slave-girl with the two thousand, then sells it for three thousand dirhams, then he gives all the profit in charity. And this is according to them, i.e., giving all the profit in charity according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, because it is filthy wealth. And Al-Shafi'i said the same in his new view, and Ahmad in one narration. (Al-Binayah: Book of Ghasp, vol. 11 p. 200)
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage deals with an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legality of profit earned from usurped (maghsūb) or deposited (wadī'ah) property when the wrongdoer trades with it. The ruling presented here is that such profit is not halal (lā yaṭību) for the usurper or depositor according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. This is based on the principle that ownership cannot be established retroactively to before the transaction because there was no valid cause (sabab al-thaman) for ownership at the time of disposition. Therefore, the act of trading is entirely in someone else's property, making the profit filthy (khabīth). The text notes that Imam Abu Yusuf disagreed, but his view is considered weaker, especially in the case of deposits. The passage further cites a detailed example from Al-Binayah where a usurper repeatedly trades and profits; the ruling is to give all profit in charity. This shows the strict Hanafi stance: any benefit derived from unauthorized use of another's property is unlawful and must be disposed of by charity, not kept by the wrongdoer.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghāṣib): A person who wrongfully takes and uses another's property without permission.
ربح خبيث (ribḥ khabīth): Profit that is legally impure and not permissible to keep; must be given in charity.
وديعة (wadī'ah): A deposit or trust left with another for safekeeping without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should the usurper do with the profit earned from trading with the usurped property, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) Keep the profit as it becomes his after the trade.
B) Give all the profit in charity.
C) Return only the original amount and keep the profit.
D) Give half the profit in charity and keep half.

Key Takeaway
Profit from unauthorized use of another's property is khabīth (filthy) and must be given in charity; the mufta bihi Hanafi position, held by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, prohibits keeping it, unlike Abu Yusuf's minority view.

6 min
The Pre-Emptor Stands in the Position of the Buyer
Establishes the principle of equivalence between pre-emptor and buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle 'al-akhdh bi al-shuf'ah bi manzilat al-shira'' to determine the options available to a pre-emptor and explain why the buyer's waiver does not bind the pre-emptor.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor is like the buyer – all buyer's rights transfer to him, but the buyer cannot waive the preemptor's rights.

This lesson examines a foundational principle of shuf'ah (pre-emption) from Al-Hidayah: the pre-emptor (shafi') is legally equivalent to the buyer (mushtari). This analogy (qiyas) extends the buyer's options – inspection and defect – to the pre-emptor, ensuring he is not disadvantaged by the original sale. We will unpack the reasoning, apply it to concrete scenarios, and highlight the critical exception that the buyer's waiver of defects does not affect the pre-emptor.

Guided Path
1
1. State the Principle
Clearly articulate the maxim: 'Taking by pre-emption is equivalent to purchasing.' This means the shafi' occupies the legal station of the mushtari.
The principle is a type of qiyas jali (clear analogy).
2
2. Identify the Legal Consequences
Recognize that all rights and obligations of a buyer in a sale contract attach to the pre-emptor. Focus on the two specific options mentioned: khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) and khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect).
These options allow the pre-emptor to rescind if the property was unseen or defective.
3
3. Determine the Conditions for Each Option
For khiyar al-ru'yah, the pre-emptor must not have seen the property at the time of taking. For khiyar al-'ayb, a hidden defect must be present. Both options are exercised upon discovery.
The text explicitly mentions these conditions.
4
4. Apply the Exception Regarding Waiver
Note that the original buyer's waiver of defects (bara'ah) does not bind the pre-emptor because the buyer is not the pre-emptor's agent and cannot waive his rights.
This protects the pre-emptor from being forced to accept a defective property.
5
5. Synthesize the Full Ruling
Combine the above: The shafi' has the right to return the property due to defect or lack of inspection, regardless of any stipulations in the original sale. This is deduced from the analogical reasoning.
The proof is the qiyas: the shafi' is like the buyer.

(1) الأخُذُ بِالشُّفْعَةِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الشَّرَاءِ.
ترجمہ شفع کے ذریعہ لینا یہ شراء کے ذریعے لینے کی مانند ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ شفعہ کے ذریعہ گھر یا زمین کو لینا ایسا ہی ہے جیسا کہ مشتری بائع سے شراء کے ذریعے لیتا ہے یعنی شفیع بمنزلہ مشتری کے ہے، لہذا جو احکامات شراء کے ہیں وہ شفعہ میں بھی ملحوظ ہوں گی۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکور و اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ جس طرح دیکھے بغیر کوئی چیز خرید نے کے بعد اس میں عیب نظر آنے کی صورت میں مشتری کو خیار عیب اور خیاور ویت ملتا ہے ٹھیک اسی طرح جب قاضی شفعہ کی بنیاد پر شفیع کے لئے گھر کا فیصلہ کر لے اور شفیع نے گھر نہ دیکھا ہوتو شفیع کو بھی خیار رؤیت حاصل ہوگا، اسی طرح عیب کی وجہ سے شفیع واپس بھی کر سکتا ہے، اگر چہ مشتری نے عیوب سے براءت کی شرط لگائی ہو، کیونکہ شفیع بمنزلہ مشتری کے ہے اور مشتری نے اگر چہ گھر دیکھا نہ ہو تو بھی اس کو خیار رؤیت حاصل ہوتا ہے، لہذا شفیع کو بھی خیار رویت حاصل ہوگا، اسی طرح مشتری کو خیار عیب حاصل ہوتا ہے، لہذ ا شفیع کو بھی خیار عیب حاصل ہوگا۔ الهداية كتاب الشفعة، باب طلب الشفعة والخصومة فيها، ج ۳ ص ۳۹۸ (قوله: وإذا قضى القاضى للشفيع بالدار ولم يكن رآها فله خيار الرؤية) لأن الشفيع بمنزلة المشترى فكما يجوز للمشترى أن يردها بخيار الرؤية، والعيب فكذا الشفيع (قوله وإن وجد بها عيبا فله أن يردها، وإن كان المشترى شرط البراءة منه لأن المشترى ليس بنائب عنه فلا يملك إسقاط حق الشفيع.
(الجوهرة النيرة كتاب الشفعة، ح ا ص ۲۸۴
Taking by pre-emption is equivalent to purchasing. Translation: Taking through shuf'ah is like taking through purchase. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that taking a house or land through shuf'ah is exactly like the buyer takes from the seller, i.e., the pre-emptor is in the position of the buyer. Therefore, the rulings that apply to purchase will also be considered in shuf'ah. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that just as a buyer gets the option of defect and option of inspection if he buys without seeing and then finds a defect, likewise when the judge decides the house in favor of the pre-emptor based on shuf'ah, and the pre-emptor had not seen the house, then the pre-emptor also gets the option of inspection. Similarly, he can return the house due to a defect, even if the buyer had stipulated a waiver of defects, because the pre-emptor is in the position of the buyer, and the buyer gets the option of inspection even if he did not see the house. So the pre-emptor also gets the option of inspection. Similarly, the buyer gets the option of defect, so the pre-emptor also gets the option of defect. Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Shuf'ah, Bab Talab al-Shuf'ah wa al-Khusumah fiha, vol. 3, p. 398 (His statement: And when the judge decrees the house for the pre-emptor and he had not seen it, he has the option of inspection) because the pre-emptor is in the position of the buyer; just as it is permissible for the buyer to return by option of inspection and defect, so too the pre-emptor. (His statement: And if he finds a defect in it, he may return it, even if the buyer had stipulated waiver from it) because the buyer is not his agent, so he cannot waive the right of the pre-emptor. (Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah, Kitab al-Shuf'ah, vol. 1, p. 284)
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage lays down the foundational principle of shuf'ah (pre-emption) that the pre-emptor (shafi') holds the same legal standing as the buyer (mushtari). The commentary explains that all rulings applicable to a buyer in a sale transaction, such as the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) and the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb), equally apply to the pre-emptor. Importantly, even if the buyer had stipulated a waiver of defects (bara'ah), the pre-emptor is not bound by that, as the buyer is not his agent. The proof is analogical: the shafi' is like the buyer. This principle is from Al-Hidayah and is supported by Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property from the buyer at the same price.
الشفيع (al-shafi'): The person entitled to pre-empt the property, standing in the position of the buyer.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection, allowing the buyer (or pre-emptor) to rescind the sale if the property was not seen at the time of contract.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option of defect, allowing the buyer (or pre-emptor) to return the property if a hidden defect is discovered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle that 'al-akhdh bi al-shuf'ah bi manzilat al-shira'', what options does the pre-emptor have analogous to the buyer?
A) Khiyar al-majlis and khiyar al-shart
B) Khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ru'yah
C) Khiyar al-tayy wa khiyar al-istihsan
D) Khiyar al-ghabn and khiyar al-'ayn

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor is legally equivalent to the buyer in the sale, thus inheriting the buyer's options of inspection and defect, and is not bound by the buyer's waiver.

Rescission of Sale Does Not Nullify Pre-emptor's Right
Ruling with proof via qiyas and example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that rescission of sale does not nullify the pre-emptor's right and apply it to a price dispute scenario.

Core Idea
A pre-emptor's right survives even if the original sale is rescinded — he takes at the seller's price.

This lesson examines a key principle from Hanafi fiqh: pre-emption (shuf'ah) is an independent right that does not depend on the continued existence of the sale contract. Even if the seller and buyer rescind their transaction due to a dispute or other reason, the pre-emptor (shafi') may still enforce his right. The ruling is supported by analogy (qiyas) with the buyer's options of sight and defect, reinforcing the strength of the pre-emptor's claim.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Read the first line: 'Rescission of the sale does not necessitate the nullification of the pre-emptor's right.' Commit this to memory.
The principle is absolute; no exception is mentioned.
2
Understand the price dispute scenario
When the seller and buyer dispute the price, both take oaths. If one refuses, the one who swears is believed; the pre-emptor takes at that price. If both swear, the judge rescinds the sale, but the pre-emptor still takes at the seller's stated price.
Rescission here is judicial (faskh), not mutual rescission (iqalah).
3
Recognize the analogy between pre-emptor and buyer
The text says: 'al-shafi' ma'a al-mushtari bimanzilat al-mushtari' — the pre-emptor stands in the position of the buyer. Therefore, he inherits the same rights, such as options to return the property.
This qiyas is jali (clear) because the illa is evident.
4
Apply the options to the pre-emptor
Just as a buyer can return a property due to defect (khiyar al-'ayb) or not having seen it (khiyar al-ru'yah), the pre-emptor can also return it after taking possession.
The pre-emptor exercises these options against the seller, not the buyer.
5
Conclude the effect of rescission
Since the pre-emptor's right is independent, rescission does not nullify it. The pre-emptor retains his right and may take the property at the price determined by the seller's oath or the seller's statement after both swear.
This protects the pre-emptor from collusion between seller and buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر بائع اور مشتری کے درمیان بیچ شیخ ہو جائے تو اس سے شفیع کا حق باطل نہ ہوگا بلکہ شفیع کو بدستور شفعہ کا حق حاصل ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر بائع اور مشتری کاشن میں اختلاف ہو جائے اور بائع مشتری سے زیادہ شمن کا دعوی کرے تو دونوں قسمیں کھائیں گے، اگر کسی ایک نے قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا تو جس نے قسم کھائی اس کے قول کا اعتبار ہوگا، لہذا شفیع اسی قیمت پر گھر لے گا اور اگر دونوں نے قسمیں کھا لیں تو قاضی بیچ کو فتح کر دے اور شفیع بائع کے قول کے مطابق شفعہ کے ذریعہ گھر لے گا، کیونکہ مذکورہ صورت میں قاضی نے بائع اور مشتری کے درمیان بیچ کو ضبح کردیا اور صبح پیچ شفیع کے حق کو باطل نہیں کر سکتا، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی فسخ بیع سے شفیع کا حق باطل نہ ہو گا۔

شرط البراءة منه أى من العيب لأن الأخذ بالشفعة بمنزلة الشراء). البناية كتاب الشفعة، باب طلب الشفعة والخصومة فيها، ج ا ا ص ٣٢٠) الهداية كتاب الشفعة، باب طلب الشفعة والخصومة فيها، فصل في الاختلاف، ج ۴ ص ۳۹۹ وَلَوِ اخْتَلَفَ الْبَائِعُ مَعَ الْمُشْتَرِى وَالشَّفِيعِ وَالدَّارُ فِي يَدِ الْبَائِعِ أَوْ الْمُشْتَرِى
(2) The rescission of the sale does not necessitate the nullification of the pre-emptor's right. Translation: The annulment of the sale does not prove the nullification of the pre-emptor's right. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that if the sale between the seller and buyer is rescinded, the pre-emptor's right is not nullified; rather, the pre-emptor retains his right of pre-emption. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if the seller and buyer dispute the price and the seller claims a higher price, both take oaths; if one refuses, the one who swears is believed, and the pre-emptor takes the house at that price. If both swear, the judge rescinds the sale, and the pre-emptor takes the house at the seller's stated price, because the rescission does not nullify the pre-emptor's right. (He said: When the pre-emptor is granted the house and he had not seen it, he has the option of sight) meaning Al-Quduri said: 'and he had not' meaning he had not seen it before. (And if he finds a defect in it, he may return it) meaning if the pre-emptor finds a defect in the house, he may return it, because the pre-emptor is in the position of the buyer vis-à-vis the seller; then the buyer can return due to the option of sight and defect, so likewise the pre-emptor can return by both options to the one from whom he took. Even if the buyer stipulated exemption from the defect, because taking by pre-emption is like a purchase. (Al-Binayah, Book of Pre-emption, Chapter on Claiming Pre-emption and Litigation, vol. 1, p. 320; Al-Hidayah, Book of Pre-emption, Chapter on Claiming Pre-emption and Litigation, Section on Disputes, vol. 4, p. 399) And if the seller disputes with the buyer and the pre-emptor, and the house is in the possession of the seller or the buyer...
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents a legal principle (Qawida) from the Hanafi school regarding pre-emption (shuf'ah). The principle states: Rescission of the sale does not nullify the pre-emptor's right. The commentary explains that even if the original sale between the seller and buyer is rescinded due to a dispute over price, the pre-emptor (shafi') still retains his right to take the property by pre-emption. The text then provides an excerpt from Al-Hidayah and Al-Binayah to support this, establishing that the pre-emptor stands in the position of the buyer, inheriting the options of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) and defect (khiyar al-'ayb). Therefore, just as the buyer can return the property upon discovering a defect or not having seen it, the pre-emptor can also return it. This analogy (qiyas) is based on the maxim that taking by pre-emption is like a purchase (al-akhdh bi al-shuf'ah bimanzilat al-shira'). The discussion concludes with a scenario where the seller, buyer, and pre-emptor dispute the price, and the ruling that the pre-emptor takes at the seller's stated price if the buyer refuses to swear, or at the seller's statement after both swear and the sale is rescinded.

Key Terms
شَفِيع (shafi'): The person who has the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) to take the sold property from the buyer.
فَسْخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, here specifically the sale between seller and buyer.
شُفْعَة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property by paying the price to the buyer.
خِيَار الرُّؤْيَة (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of sight, allowing a buyer who bought a property without seeing it to cancel the sale upon seeing it.
خِيَار الْعَيْب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option of defect, allowing a buyer to return a defective item if the defect was unknown at purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The pre-emptor's right is nullified because the sale no longer exists.
B) The pre-emptor loses his right only if the rescission is by mutual consent.
C) The pre-emptor's right remains valid, and he may take the property at the price stated by the seller.
D) The pre-emptor must renegotiate with the seller after the rescission.

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor's right is independent; rescission of the sale does not nullify it.

Reduction of Part of Price Attaches to Contract
States a legal principle of pre-emption with proof and application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a partial reduction of the price by the seller attaches to the original contract, and distinguish it from a full reduction, in pre-emption cases.

Core Idea
A partial price reduction retroactively defines the contract price; a full waiver does not.

In pre-emption (shuf'ah), the pre-emptor steps into the buyer's shoes. But what happens if the seller later reduces the price? This lesson explains a key Hanafi rule: a reduction of part of the price is treated as if it were part of the original contract, so the pre-emptor benefits. However, if the seller forgives the entire price, the pre-emptor is not excused. This distinction is crucial for calculating the pre-emptor's obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the seller's reduction of part of the price after the sale. The ruling is that it attaches to the original contract (yaltaḥiqu bi aṣl al-ʿaqd).
This is a legal principle, not a physical act.
2
Locate the dalil
The source cites the principle from Al-Hidayah and supports it with quotes from Badai' al-Sanai' and Al-Inayah. The proof is the established Hanafi maxim and its consistent application.
The dalil is ijmāʿ of the Hanafi school on this rule.
3
Apply to the pre-emptor
The pre-emptor (shafi') takes the property at the reduced price because the reduction is deemed part of the original contract. The pre-emptor pays what remains of the price.
The reduction applies to the pre-emptor as if it were agreed at the time of sale.
4
Contrast with full reduction
If the seller forgives the entire price (hatt al-kull), it does not attach; the pre-emptor must still pay the full price. The full waiver is a separate act of charity, not part of the sale.
Only partial reduction is retroactive; full reduction is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب طلب الشفعة والخصومة فيها
(۳) حَطَّ الْبَعْضِ يَلْتَحِقُ بِأَصْلِ الْعَقْدِ بِخِلَافِ حَطِ الْكُلّ. ترجمہ بعض شمن کا کم کرنا اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق ہوگا بر خلاف کل شمن کے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر عقد کے بعد بائع مشتری سے شمن میں سے کچھ من کم کر دے تو یہ کی اصل عقد کے ساتھ ملحق ہوگی، یعنی گویا کہ اصل عقد اسی شمن پر واقع ہوا تھا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر بائع نے مشتری سے بعض شمن کم کر دیا تو یہ شفیع سے بھی ساقط ہوگا کیونکہ ثمن وہی ہے جو باقی ہے، یعنی شفیع بھی وہی کم شمن دے کر دار مشفوعہ کو لے گا، اور اگر بائع نے کل ثمن چھوڑ دیا تو شفیع سے کل ثمن ساقط نہ ہوگا، کیونکہ بعض شمن کی کمی اصل عقد سے ملحق ہوتی ہے مکمل شمن کی نہیں، پید ا شفیع کے حق میں لَكِنَّهُ لَمْ يَنْقُدُ الثَّمَنَ فَالْقَوْلُ فِي ذَلِكَ قَوْلُ الْبَائِعِ، وَالْبَائِعُ مَعَ الْمُشْتَرِى يَتَحَالَفَانِ وَيَتَرَادَّان، وَالشَّفِيعُ يَأْخُذُ الدَّارَ بِمَا قَالَ الْبَائِعُ إِنْ شَاءَ. (بدائع الصنائع: كتاب الشفعة، فصل في اختلاف الشفيع والمشتري، ج ۵ ص ۳۱) قَالَ (وَإِذَا ادَّعَى الْمُشْتَرِى ثَمَنًا وَادَّعَى الْبَائِعُ أَقَلَّ مِنْهُ إِلَخَ) إِذَا اخْتَلَفَ الْبَائِعُ وَالْمُشْتَرِى فِى الثَّمَنِ فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ مَقْبُوضًا أَوْ غَيْرَ مَقْبُوضٍ، أَوْ يَكُونَ الْقَبْضُ غَيْرَ ظَاهِرٍ : يَعْنِي غَيْرَ مَعْلُومٍ لِلشَّفِيعِ، فَإِنْ كَانَ غَيْرَ مَقْبُوضٍ فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَدَّعِيَ الْبَائِعُ أَقَلَّ أَوْ أَكْثَرَ ، فَإِنْ كَانَ أَقَلَّ أَخَذَهَا الشَّفِيعُ بِمَا قَالَ الْبَائِعُ وَكَانَ ذَلِكَ حَقًّا عَنِ الْمُشْتَرِى. (العناية كتاب الشفعة، فصل في الاختلاف، ج ۹ ص ۳۹۲) الهداية كتاب الشفعة طلب الشفعة والخصومة فيها، فصل فيما يوخذ به المشفوع، ج۴ ص ۴۰۰
Chapter on Claiming Pre-emption and Litigation Therein
(3) The reduction of part attaches to the original contract, unlike the reduction of the whole. Translation: Reducing part of the price will attach to the original contract, contrary to reducing the whole price. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that if after the contract the seller reduces something from the price for the buyer, it will be annexed to the original contract, i.e., as if the original contract was concluded at that reduced price. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if the seller reduces part of the price for the buyer, this reduction also applies to the pre-emptor, because the price is what remains; i.e., the pre-emptor will also take the pre-empted house at that reduced price. But if the seller forgives the entire price, the whole price is not dropped for the pre-emptor, because reduction of part attaches to the original contract, but reduction of the whole does not. Hence, regarding the pre-emptor's right: but he did not pay the price, so the word in that is the seller's word, and the seller and buyer take oaths and mutually return, and the pre-emptor takes the house at what the seller says if he wishes. (Badai' al-Sanai': Book of Pre-emption, Section on Dispute between Pre-emptor and Buyer, vol. 5, p. 31) He said: (When the buyer claims a price and the seller claims less than it, etc.) When the seller and buyer dispute over the price, it may be that the price has been paid or not, or payment is unclear, meaning unknown to the pre-emptor. If it has not been paid, and the seller claims less, the pre-emptor takes the house at what the seller says, and that is a right against the buyer. (Al-Inayah, Book of Pre-emption, Section on Disputes, vol. 9, p. 392; Al-Hidayah, Book of Pre-emption, Chapter on Claiming Pre-emption and Litigation Therein, Section on What is Taken by Pre-emption, vol. 4, p. 400)
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents a second legal principle regarding pre-emption (shuf'ah): The reduction of part of the price by the seller to the buyer attaches to the original contract, whereas a reduction of the entire price does not. The commentary explains that if the seller reduces a portion of the price, the pre-emptor (shafi') benefits from this reduction because the reduced price is considered as if it were the original contract price. Thus, the pre-emptor pays only the reduced amount. However, if the seller completely forgives the price (i.e., gives the property for free), the pre-emptor is not absolved from paying; he must still pay the full price because a total reduction does not attach to the original contract in the same way. The text then quotes Badai' al-Sanai' and Al-Hidayah to illustrate this rule in the context of a dispute between seller and buyer over the price, where the seller's word is accepted if the price has not been paid, and the pre-emptor takes the house at the seller's stated price. The rationale is that the original contract is deemed to have been concluded at the agreed price at the time of contract, but a post-contract partial reduction is retroactive, while a full waiver is a separate remission.

Key Terms
حَطّ (hatt): Reduction or remission of part or all of the price by the seller to the buyer after the contract.
الْتَحَقَ بِأَصْل الْعَقْد (iltaḥaqa bi aṣl al-'aqd): To be annexed to or treated as part of the original contract retroactively.
ثَمَن (thaman): Price paid in a sale transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle, if a seller reduces part of the price for the buyer after the contract, what is the effect on the pre-emptor (shafi')?
A) The pre-emptor must pay the reduced price because the reduction attaches to the original contract.
B) The pre-emptor must still pay the original price because the reduction is a new contract.
C) The pre-emptor is freed from paying anything if the reduction is more than half.
D) The pre-emptor has the option to accept the reduction or not.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi pre-emption law, a partial reduction of the price is retroactively part of the contract, but a full forgiveness is not.

Seller and His Agent Lose the Right of Pre-emption (Shuf'a)
States and explains the shuf'a principle for sellers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the seller and his agent lose the right of pre-emption (shuf'a), and explain the legal reasoning based on disinterest (i'rad).

Core Idea
The seller's act of selling is evidence of disinterest, which invalidates the weak right of shuf'a.

In this lesson, we examine a fundamental principle in Hanafi law of pre-emption: the seller and his agent lose the right to claim shuf'a, while the buyer and his agent retain it. The reasoning hinges on the nature of shuf'a as a weak right that is voided by disinterest (i'rad). We will explore the legal logic and its application to agents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the principle: 'Whoever sells or is sold for, there is no pre-emption for him, contrary to purchase.' Identify that this means the seller and his agent lose shuf'a.
2
Identify the Parties Involved
Recognize four categories: seller, seller's agent, buyer, buyer's agent. The ruling treats seller and his agent the same; buyer and his agent the same.
3
Analyze the Legal Reasoning (Talil)
The act of selling (or causing sale) indicates disinterest (i'rad) in the property. Shuf'a is a weak right (haqq da'if) that is invalidated by disinterest.
The text contrasts with the buyer's purchase, which shows desire (raghbah) and thus preserves shuf'a.
4
Apply to the Agent Scenario
If the seller's agent also happens to be a pre-emptor (shafi'), his own act of selling on behalf of the seller demonstrates disinterest, so he loses his right to pre-empt the same property.
The agent cannot claim shuf'a because he is the one who executed the sale from the seller's side.
5
Contrast with the Buyer's Agent
The buyer's agent, by purchasing, shows desire and thus retains the right of shuf'a. He may exercise it if he is otherwise entitled.
The distinction is based on the presence (buyer) or absence (seller) of desire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول ہدایہ رائع

ترجمہ: جس نے مکان فروخت کیا یا جس کے لئے فروخت کیا گیا تو اس کو حق شفعہ
حاصل نہ ہوگا۔

مشفوعہ زمین میں رغبت ہے، شفعہ تو اعراض کرنے سے باطل ہوتا ہے نہ کہ رغبت سے۔
ورد العوض..... لأن حق الشفعة ليس بحق متقرر فى المحل) يعني أن الشفيع ليس له ملک فی المحل بل له حق التعرض بالملک، فتسليمه الشفعة يكون ترك العوض منه. (البناية: كتاب الشفعة، باب ما يبطل به الشفعة، ج ۱۱ ص ۳۶۹) الهداية كتاب الشفعة، باب ما تبطل به الشفعة، ج ۴ ص ۴۰۸
