Principles of Hidayah (199) (8) Whoever sells or is sold for, there is no pre-emption for him, contrary to purchase. Translation: The one who sold a house or for whom it was sold, he will not have the right of pre-emption. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that if the seller sold land or a house, or the seller's agent sold, in both cases the seller will not have the right of pre-emption, because the seller's selling or the seller's agent's selling is evidence of disinterest from the property, and pre-emption is a weak right that becomes void by disinterest, contrary to the buyer's purchase or the buyer's agent's purchase. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if the seller's agent sells the house or land and this same agent is also the pre-emptor, then he will not have the right of pre-emption, because the seller's agent himself sold the house or land, and whoever sells the property subject to pre-emption does not have the right of pre-emption, because he is undoing a transaction that was completed from his side. Therefore, if the seller's agent is the pre-emptor, he also has no right of pre-emption. On the contrary, if the buyer's agent wants to exercise pre-emption, he can do so, because his taking by pre-emption is like a purchase, and he has desire for the property subject to pre-emption. Pre-emption becomes void by disinterest, not by desire. And the return of consideration... because the right of pre-emption is not a fixed right in the property, meaning the pre-emptor has no ownership in the property, but he has the right to assert ownership, so his relinquishing of pre-emption is leaving the consideration. (Al-Binayah: Book of Pre-emption, Chapter of What Invalidates Pre-emption, vol. 11 p. 369) Al-Hidayah: Book of Pre-emption, Chapter of What Invalidates Pre-emption, vol. 4 p. 408) (And the agent of the seller when he sells and he is the pre-emptor, there is no pre-emption for him, etc. He mentioned the principle, which is that whoever...
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This lesson presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the right of pre-emption (shuf'a). The passage begins with the principle: 'Whoever sells or is sold for, there is no pre-emption for him, contrary to purchase.' The author expands on this principle, explaining that if a person sells property (or has it sold on his behalf through an agent), he loses the right of pre-emption. The reasoning is that the act of selling demonstrates disinterest (i'rad) in the property, and since shuf'a is a weak right, it is invalidated by such disinterest. In contrast, a buyer (or his agent) who purchases the property retains the right of pre-emption because his act of purchasing shows desire (raghbah) for the property, and shuf'a is lost only through disinterest, not desire. The passage then cites a longer Arabic text from a commentary clarifying the nature of shuf'a: it is not a fixed right of ownership but a right to assert ownership, so waiving it is like giving up consideration. The passage also quotes Al-Hidayah and Al-Binayah as sources. The key lesson is that the seller and his agent are treated as having relinquished shuf'a by their own action of selling, while the buyer and his agent are not.

Key Terms
شُفْعَة (shuf'a): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to pre-empt a sale and take the property by paying the purchase price.
بَائِع (bā'i'): The seller of the property; the party who transfers ownership.
مُشْتَرِي (mushtarī): The buyer of the property; the party who acquires ownership.
إِعْرَاض (i'rāḍ): Disinterest or turning away from the property, which invalidates the right of shuf'a.
رَغْبَة (raghbah): Desire or interest in the property, which preserves the right of shuf'a.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle stated in the passage, who among the following loses the right of pre-emption (shuf'a)?
A) The buyer who purchases the property
B) The seller who sells the property
C) The agent of the buyer who purchases on behalf of the buyer
D) The co-owner who did not sell

Key Takeaway
The seller and his agent lose the right of pre-emption due to disinterest, while the buyer and his agent retain it due to desire.

The Principle that One Person Cannot Be Both Plaintiff and Defendant
States the maxim that one person cannot be both plaintiff and defendant, with application to partition law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that a single person cannot be both plaintiff and defendant, and apply it to partition cases involving a sole heir.

Core Idea
A lawsuit requires two opposing parties — one person cannot sue himself.

This lesson introduces a fundamental rule of Hanafi litigation procedure: a single individual cannot occupy both roles of plaintiff and defendant. The principle ensures the adversarial structure necessary for a valid case. We will see its application in partition (qismah) where only one heir exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
State the maxim in Arabic and English. Understand the terms mukhasim (plaintiff) and mukhasam (defendant).
The maxim is 'al-wahid la yasluhu mukhasiman wa mukhasaman.'
2
Understand the rational proof
Why can't one person be both? Because the same person would be claiming against himself, which is logically impossible (dalil aqli).
The proof is reason itself; no text is needed for this self-evident rule.
3
Apply to partition
If there is only one heir, he cannot sue himself for partition. He must use another legal mechanism, such as requesting the court to appoint a representative or seeking voluntary division.
The court will not hear a case where the same person is both litigants.
4
Note the cross-reference
The passage links to the earlier rule: the judge does not compel a single qasim (partitioner), reinforcing that rights are not forced.
This connection shows how the maxim operates in practical fiqh.
5
Practical consequence
A petition in which the same person is both plaintiff and defendant is invalid and will be dismissed.
The case is void ab initio due to lack of proper parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ: ایک ہی آدمی مخاصم اور مخاصم بننے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا۔
تشریح مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ایک ہی آدمی خصومت بھی کرے اور اس سے خصومت بھی کی جائے اس کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا، یعنی شخص واحد مدعی بھی ہو اور مدعی علیہ بھی ہو ایسا نہیں ہوسکتا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر ورثاء میں سے صرف ایک

Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that one person cannot both conduct litigation and have litigation conducted against him; that is, a single person cannot be both plaintiff and defendant. (And the judge does not compel people to a single partitioner; this is the wording of al-Quduri in his Mukhtasar. (Meaning) i.e. the meaning of al-Quduri's statement: he does not compel them to hire him) i.e. the judge does not compel the co-partitioners to hire a specific partitioner (because there is no compulsion on contracts, for the right is theirs; so if they agree on someone to execute their right, it is permissible just as in other rights. Commentary
This passage introduces another legal maxim: 'One person cannot be both a litigant (mukhasim) and the one litigated against (mukhasam).' In plain terms, a single individual cannot be both plaintiff and defendant in the same case. This principle is rooted in the structure of legal proceedings, which require two distinct parties. The author applies it to the scenario of inheritance and partition: if there is only one heir, that person cannot initiate a partition case against himself. He would need to either appoint a representative or the court would handle matters differently. The passage then reiterates the earlier point about the judge not compelling a single qasim, but this is likely a textual repetition or the OCR misplacing the quote. The core lesson is that legal disputes require opposing parties; one person cannot fulfill both roles.

Key Terms
مخاصم (mukhasim): A litigant, one who brings a lawsuit (plaintiff).
مخاصم (mukhasam): The one against whom litigation is brought (defendant).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The judge can force the single heir to appoint a co-heir from outside.
D) The maxim does not apply to partition cases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim that one person cannot be both plaintiff and defendant is a foundational rule ensuring the integrity of the adversarial process in Islamic courts.

The Ruling: Judge Cannot Compel a Specific Divider
Ruling with two proofs from Hanafi texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the judge cannot compel co-heirs to hire a specific divider and identify the two reasons given in Al-Hidayah.

Core Idea
The judge cannot force a divider because hiring is a contract requiring mutual consent.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle from the Hanafi school regarding property division. The source passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies that a judge does not have the authority to compel co-owners or co-heirs to hire a particular person to divide their jointly owned property. This ruling is based on two foundational legal principles: freedom of contract and the prohibition of causing harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Find the central claim: the judge cannot force people to use a single divider. Note that this is a negative ruling (prohibition on the judge's action).
The phrase 'la yujbir' indicates a prohibition of compulsion.
2
Understand the nature of the act
Recognize that hiring a divider is a contract (aqd). The judge's compulsion would override the voluntary nature of contracts.
Contracts require consent of both parties; compulsion invalidates them.
3
Extract the first proof
The first proof is 'la jabr 'ala al-uqud' (no compulsion in contracts). This is a general principle: the judge cannot compel any contract, including hiring a divider.
This proof is textually cited in the passage.
4
Extract the second proof
The second proof is based on harm: if only one divider were appointed, he could demand excessive wages, causing harm to the parties. The principle 'la darar wa la dirar' (no harm in Islam) applies.
This is a harm-prevention rationale.
5
Confirm the author's reliance
Note that the passage cites Al-Quduri and Al-Hidayah, and the commentators Al-Binaya and Al-Lubab affirm this as the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
This establishes the weight of the ruling within the Hanafi school.

Source Text

اصول ہدا یہ رائج
۲۰۱
احب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ قاضی لوگوں کو کسی ایک متعین قاسم یعنی تقسیم کرنے والے کو بطور اجرت لینے پر مجبور نہیں کرسکتا، کیونکہ یہ عقد ہے، اور عقود میں قاضی لوگوں کو مجبور نہیں کر سکتا ، لہذا قاضی ایک ہی قاسم پر بھی لوگوں کو مجبور نہیں کر سکتا۔ نیز ایک ہی قاسم کو متعین کرنے کی صورت میں لوگوں کا نقصان ہوگا کہ قاسم لوگوں کی مجبوری کا فائدہ اٹھا کر زیادہ اجرت کا مطالبہ کرے گا۔ ( ولا يجبر القاضى الناس على قاسم واحد هذا لفظ القدوري في مختصره (مـعـنـاه) أي مـعـنـى كــلام القدوري هذا لا يجبرهم على أن يستأجروه) أى لا يجبر القاضى المتقاسمين على استئجار قاسم معين (لأنه لا جبر على العقود لأن الحق لهم، فإذا رضوا بمن يتولى حقهم جاز كما في سائر الحقوق. ولأنه لو تعين أى ولأن القاسم الواحد لو تعين التحكم بالزيادة على أجر مثله) وفيه ضرر عليهم ولا ضرر في الإسلام. (البناية: كتاب القسمة، ح ا ا ص ۴۰۴) ولا يجبر القاضى الناس على قاسم (واحد قال في الهداية معناه لا يجبرهم على أن يستأجروه، لأنه لا جبر على العقود، ولأنه لو تعين لتحكيم بالزيادة على أجر مثله اللباب) في شرح الكتاب: كتاب القسمة، ج ۴ ص ۹۲) الهداية كتاب القسمة، ج ۴ ص ۴۱۴
Principles of Hidayah, page 201. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that the judge cannot compel people to hire a specific divider (qasim) for a fee, because this is a contract, and in contracts the judge cannot compel people. Therefore, the judge cannot compel people to use a single divider. Also, if a single divider were appointed, people would be harmed because the divider would exploit their compulsion and demand excessive wages. (The judge does not compel people to [use] a single divider — this is the wording of al-Quduri in his Mukhtasar. Its meaning: he does not compel them to hire him. That is, the judge does not compel the co-partitioners to hire a specific divider, because there is no compulsion in contracts, as the right is theirs. So if they consent to whomever handles their right, it is permissible, as in all other rights. And because if [the divider] were appointed, he would become tyrannical by demanding more than his fair wage, and therein is harm to them, and there is no harm in Islam.) (Al-Binaya: Kitab al-Qismah, vol. 4, p. 404; Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Qismah, vol. 4, p. 414; Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab: Kitab al-Qismah, vol. 4, p. 92).
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi text. The principle is that the judge cannot force co-heirs or co-owners to hire a specific person to divide their property. The reason is that hiring a divider is a contract (aqd), and Islamic law does not allow compulsion in contracts because the right belongs to the parties themselves. If they agree on someone, it is valid. Additionally, if only one divider were designated, the divider might take advantage of the compulsion and demand more than the fair wage, which would harm the parties. The principle of 'no harm in Islam' (la darar wa la dirar fil-Islam) underpins this. The passage cites Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar and provides explanations from Al-Binaya and Al-Hidayah, confirming this as the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
قاسم (qāsim): The person appointed to divide property among co-owners or co-heirs.
عقود (ʿuqūd): Contracts; legal agreements that require mutual consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school prohibit the judge from forcing co-heirs to hire a specific divider?
A) Because the divider must be a relative of the deceased.
B) Because contracts require mutual consent and compulsion causes harm.
C) Because the judge lacks jurisdiction in property matters.
D) Because the divider must be appointed by the heirs themselves.

Key Takeaway
The judge cannot compel co-heirs to hire a specific divider because hiring is a contract (requiring consent) and forced appointment leads to harm.

One Person Cannot Be Both Plaintiff and Defendant
States principle that one person cannot be both opponent and opposed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedural rule that one person cannot be both opponent and opposed, and apply it to determine when a judge may proceed with estate partition.

Core Idea
A single person cannot simultaneously be plaintiff and defendant — two distinct opponents are essential for a valid judicial proceeding.

This lesson examines a fundamental principle of Hanafi judicial procedure: the necessity of two distinct parties in litigation. The author of Al-Hidayah uses the context of estate partition to illustrate that a judge cannot proceed if only one heir appears, because that heir would have to act as both claimant and defendant, which is procedurally invalid. Understanding this rule ensures fair representation of all heirs and prevents conflicts of interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is requesting judicial partition of an estate. The ruling: if only one heir appears before the judge, the judge does not divide the property, even if the heir produces evidence.
The ruling is specific to the judge's action — he cannot proceed, not that the partition is haram.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the legal principle 'al-wahid la yasluhu mukhasiman wa mukhasaman' (the single person is not fit to be both opponent and opposed). This is a maxim derived from the nature of litigation requiring two adversaries.
The proof is rational (i'idal) rather than textual, but firmly established in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The principle logically implies that in any dispute, two distinct parties must present their arguments. If only one person appears, they cannot fulfill both roles; thus, the proceeding cannot commence. The judge cannot create a second party from the same individual.
This is a structural requirement, not a discretionary rule.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools may allow partition if the absent heirs are lawfully represented (e.g., by power of attorney). The Hanafi school insists on physical presence of two separate individuals as opponents, even if representation is available.
The Hanafi view is stricter to prevent the same person from dominating both sides.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, a Hanafi judge will dismiss the case and require at least two heirs to appear before proceeding. Another school might allow the present heir to represent all and proceed, potentially disadvantaging absent heirs.
The practical consequence affects the speed and fairness of estate distribution.

(العناية: كتاب القسمة، ج ۹ ص ۴۳۴) (قوله وإن حضر وارث واحد لم يقسم وإن أقام البينة لأنه لا بد من حضور خصمين لأن الواحد لا يصلح مخاصما ومخاصما فكذا مقاسما ومقاسما . (الجوهرة النيرة كتاب القسمة، ج ۲ ص ۲۴۹ الهداية كتاب القسمة، ج ۴ ص ۴۱۸
(10) One person is not fit to be both opponent and opposed. Translation: A single individual is not capable of being both the litigant and the one litigated against. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that one person cannot simultaneously conduct litigation and be the subject of litigation from the same proceeding, i.e., one person cannot be both plaintiff and defendant. The author of Hidayah states this principle to explain that if only one heir appears before the judge and requests partition, the judge will not divide, even if this heir establishes proof, because for a decision on division, the presence of two opponents is necessary. If the present person is considered an opponent on his own behalf, there is no opponent on behalf of the deceased or the absent one. If he is considered an opponent on their behalf, then there is no one to oppose on his own behalf to establish proof. Therefore, the judge cannot divide with only one heir present; the presence of two individuals is necessary. Similarly, one person cannot be both the divider and the one being divided. (If one heir is present, partition is not made even if he produces evidence, because two opponents are required, for one person cannot be opponent and opposed. So the present person, if he is an opponent for himself, there is no opponent for the deceased and the absent; if he is opponent for them, there is no one to oppose for himself to establish evidence.) (Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Qismah, vol. 9, p. 434; Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah: Kitab al-Qismah, vol. 2, p. 249; Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Qismah, vol. 4, p. 418).
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof stating a procedural principle in Islamic judicial proceedings: the same person cannot act as both claimant (mudda'i) and defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) in a single case. The context is partition (qisma) of an estate. If only one heir appears, the judge cannot proceed because there must be two opposing parties to represent the interests of the deceased and any absent heirs. If the lone heir acts for himself, there is no opponent for the deceased; if he acts for both sides, he becomes both opponent and opposed, which is procedurally impossible. The principle extends to division itself: the same person cannot simultaneously be the divider (qasim) and the one being divided (muqasim). This ensures fair representation and avoids conflicts of interest. The passage cites Al-'Inayah, Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah, and Al-Hidayah to reinforce this.

Key Terms
مخاصم (mukhāṣim): The person who is litigating (opponent).
مخاصمًا (mukhāṣaman): The person against whom litigation is brought (opposed).
خصم (khaṣm): Opponent in litigation; party to a dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The judge proceeds with partition if the heir produces evidence of ownership.
B) The judge does not divide because two opponents are required.
C) The judge appoints a representative for the absent heirs and proceeds.
D) The judge proceeds only if the heir is a relative of the deceased.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a judicial proceeding in Hanafi law requires two distinct parties; one person cannot simultaneously be the claimant and the defendant.

Unequal Partition by Mutual Consent
States principle that unequal division of common property is valid if all parties agree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on unequal partition and explain why mutual consent makes it permissible.

Core Idea
Unequal partition of jointly-owned property is valid if all owners consent—consent transforms it into a permissible exchange.

In Islamic property law, division of common property normally must be proportional to each owner's share. However, the Hanafi school recognizes that when all parties freely agree, they may divide the property unequally. This lesson examines the principle from Al-Hidayah and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is partition (qisma) of jointly-owned property. The ruling is that unequal division (qisma 'ala al-tafadul) is permissible (ja'iz) under a specific condition.
The ruling is conditional.
2
Identify the condition
The condition is mutual consent (taradi) of all co-owners. Without it, division must be proportional to ownership shares.
Consent is the key factor.
3
Trace the legal basis
The permissibility arises because the parties are waiving their rights voluntarily; the division becomes a form of exchange (mu'awada) by consent, which is valid under Hanafi contract law.
This is analogous to a sale or gift.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require strict proportionality even with consent, but the Hanafi school permits it based on the principle of consent overriding default rules.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that unequal partition by mutual consent is permissible, as stated in Al-Hidayah and its commentaries.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١١) الْقِسْمَةُ عَلَى التَّفَاضُلِ جَائِزٌ بِالتّرَاضِي . . ترجمہ: باہم رضامندی سے شرکاء کے مابین کمی بیشی کے ساتھ تقسیم کرنا جائز ہے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر شرکاء میں سے ایک شریک کو تقسیم میں زیادہ حصہ دیا جائے شر کاء کی رضا مندی کے ساتھ تو یہ جائز ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلار ہے ہیں کہ اگر شر کا ء نے یہ شرط لگائی کہ گھر کا راستہ دونوں کے درمیان دو تہائی ہوگا تو یہ جائز ہے، اگر چہ اصل گھر دونوں کے
(11) Partition with inequality is permissible by mutual consent. Translation: Dividing among partners with an excess or deficiency (i.e., unequal shares) is permissible if done with mutual consent. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that if one partner is given a larger share in the division with the consent of the partners, it is permissible. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if the partners stipulate that the common passage in a house will be two-thirds for one and one-third for the other, this is permissible, even though the original house belongs to both equally.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This is a ruling stating a principle in the law of partition (qisma). Normally, division must be proportional to ownership shares (qisma 'ala al-ijtima'). However, if all co-owners freely agree, they may distribute the common property in unequal shares (qisma 'ala al-tafadul). This is a form of exchange (mu'awada) by consent. The example given: partners may agree that the shared pathway in a house be divided into two-thirds for one and one-third for the other, even if the house itself is owned equally. This is valid because the parties have waived their rights voluntarily. The principle underscores the importance of mutual consent in property transactions, a cornerstone of Hanafi contract law.

Key Terms
قسمة على التفاضل (qisma ʿalā al-tafāḍul): Division where shares are not proportional to original ownership; unequal partition.
تراضی (tarāḍī): Mutual consent; agreement of all parties involved.

Key Takeaway
Unequal partition (qisma 'ala al-tafadul) is valid in the Hanafi school if and only if all co-owners freely consent, reflecting the importance of party autonomy in property transactions.

Qiyas Overridden by Public Practice: The Muzara'ah Dispute
Introducing a Hanafi usuli principle and its application to an intra-madhhab dispute over sharecropping.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that public practice (ta'amul) overrides qiyas, and apply it to explain why muzara'ah with a fixed share is permissible according to the mufta bihi within the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
When qiyas and public practice conflict, the Hanafi school follows the practice — as in muzara'ah and istisna'.

The Hanafi school is known for its extensive use of qiyas, but it also recognizes that rigid analogical reasoning cannot override the established practice of the Muslim community. This lesson examines the principle that qiyas is abandoned for ta'amul (public practice), as applied to the contract of muzara'ah (sharecropping). You will see how an internal Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples is resolved by giving weight to the continuous practice of Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
State the maxim 'al-qiyas yutraku bi al-ta'amul' and explain its meaning: when qiyas and public practice conflict, practice prevails.
This principle is key to many transactional rulings in the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
In muzara'ah (sharecropping) with a fixed share (e.g., one-third or one-fourth), Abu Hanifa rules it batil (invalid) due to qiyas, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule it ja'iz (permissible).
The dispute is over the legal effect of ta'amul.
3
Analyze the Conflict
Qiyas suggests the contract is invalid because of uncertainty (gharar) and sale of non-existent goods. However, ta'amul (continuous practice of the community) and public need require its permissibility.
Identify why qiyas and ta'amul give opposite results.
4
Conclude the Tarjih
The Sahibayn's view is adopted as mufta bihi because ta'amul overrides qiyas. The text cites the parallel of istisna' (contract manufacturing) where ta'amul also overrides qiyas.
This shows the principle's broader application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المزارعة (۱۴) الْقِيَاسُ يُتْرَكُ بِالتَّعَامُلِ . ترجمہ: تعامل کی وجہ سے قیاس کو چھوڑ دیا جائے گا۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب قیاس اور تعامل الناس کا کسی مسئلہ کے جواز اور عدم جواز میں ٹکراؤ ہو جائے تو تعامل الناس پر عمل کیا جائے گا اور قیاس کو ترک کیا جائے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ عقد مزارعت بالثلث یا بالربع امام صاحب کے نزدیک باطل ہے جبکہ صاحبین رحمہما اللہ کے نزدیک جائز ہے، اور مفتی بہ قول صاحبین کا ہے، کیونکہ لوگوں کو اس کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے اور تعامل الناس بھی ہے جبکہ قیاس کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ یہ جائز نہ ہو، مگر جب تعامل الناس اور قیاس کا ٹکراؤ ہو جائے تو تعامل الناس کی وجہ سے قیاس کو ترک کیا جاتا ہے، لہذا مزارعت کے باب میں بھی تعامل کی وجہ سے قیاس کو ترک کیا جائے گا، جیسے استصناع میں ہوتا ہے کہ آرڈر دے کر کوئی سامان بنوایا جاتا ہے، ظاہر ہے کہ اس میں معدوم کی بیچ ہے جو قیاس کی رو سے جائز نہیں ، مگر تعامل الناس کے سبب جائز ہے۔
Book of Muzara'ah (14) Qiyas is abandoned due to public practice. Translation: Qiyas will be abandoned because of 'amal. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that when there is a conflict between qiyas and the practice of the people regarding permissibility or impermissibility, the practice of the people will be acted upon and qiyas will be abandoned. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle and states that the contract of muzara'ah with a third or a fourth is invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa, while according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is permissible, and the mufta bihi is the saying of the two companions, because people have need for it and there is public practice, whereas qiyas requires that it not be permissible. But when there is a conflict between public practice and qiyas, qiyas is abandoned due to public practice. Therefore, in the chapter of muzara'ah, qiyas will also be abandoned due to practice, just as in istisna' where one orders and gets something made; obviously, that involves selling a non-existing item which is not permissible according to qiyas, but it is permissible due to public practice.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage introduces a foundational Hanafi usuli principle: public practice (ta'amul) overrides analogical reasoning (qiyas) when they conflict. The author presents this as a legal maxim and immediately applies it to the contract of muzara'ah (sharecropping). The intra-Hanafi dispute is clearly delineated: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a muzara'ah contract with a fixed share (e.g., one-third or one-fourth of the crop for the worker) is invalid (batil) because it violates qiyas—since the worker's share is uncertain and the landowner's compensation is tied to an unknown outcome. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) deem it permissible, and their view is the relied-upon position for fatwa. The justification is the continuous practice of Muslims from the time of the Prophet (PBUH) without rejection, and the necessity of such contracts. The commentator draws an analogy to istisna' (contract manufacturing), where selling a non-existent object is normally prohibited by qiyas but is permitted due to widespread practice. This principle (qiyas yutraku bi al-ta'amul) is a key tool in Hanafi jurisprudence, allowing flexibility to accommodate societal needs.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence; deducing a ruling for a new case based on the effective cause (illa) found in an original case.
التعامل (al-ta'amul): Public practice or customary dealing; the widespread and continuous practice of people that is not rejected.
المزارعة (al-muzara'ah): A sharecropping contract where one party provides land and the other works it, dividing the produce according to agreed share.
الاستصناع (al-istisna'): A contract of manufacturing a specified item where the manufacturer agrees to produce it for a given price; a sale of a non-existent object.
صاحبين (al-sahibayn): The two companions: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the primary disciples of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) preferred over Abu Hanifa's in the permissibility of muzara'ah with a share?
A) Because narrations from the Prophet (PBUH) clearly permit it.
B) Because the continuous practice of Muslims and public need override the qiyas that would prohibit it.
C) Because Abu Hanifa later retracted his opinion.
D) Because it involves a form of partnership, which is always permissible.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the continuous practice of the community (ta'amul) can override analogical reasoning (qiyas) when they conflict, as seen in the permissibility of muzara'ah with a fixed share.

The Invalidating Condition in Muzara'ah
Explaining that stipulating a fixed share voids the muzara'ah contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that voids a muzara'ah contract and explain why it destroys the partnership.

Core Idea
A fixed share guarantee voids the contract because it severs the profit-sharing partnership.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle from Al-Hidayah: any condition that ends the partnership nature of muzara'ah invalidates the contract. The example given is stipulating a fixed measure (qafiz) for one party. We will explore the rationale, the intra-Hanafi difference on basic permissibility, and the mufta bihi ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Prohibited Condition
Locate the specific condition mentioned: stipulating a fixed quantity (qafiz musamma) of produce for one party, e.g., ten manns, while dividing the rest.
The condition creates a guaranteed minimum, not a proportional share.
2
Understand the Legal Cause (Illah)
The condition invalidates because it terminates the partnership (sharika); if the crop equals only that fixed amount, nothing remains to be shared, leading to dispute.
The illah is 'qat' al-sharika' – severing the partnership.
3
Distinguish from Basic Permissibility
Note that the contract itself is ja'iz according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), though Abu Hanifa deemed muzara'ah batil. The condition is void by consensus.
The mufta bihi is the contract's permissibility, but such conditions remain invalid.
4
Apply the Principle to Other Conditions
Generalize: any condition that eliminates profit-sharing (e.g., fixed rent, guaranteed output) invalidates the contract. Conditions consistent with partnership (e.g., proportional shares) are permissible.
The principle is 'kullu shart yunhi al-sharika yubtil al-muzara'ah'.

Usool e Hidayah page 206 (15) Stipulating something that terminates the partnership invalidates muzara'ah. Translation: Conditions that end partnership will invalidate muzara'ah. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that the contract of muzara'ah becomes invalid with any condition that terminates the partnership, such as stipulating a fixed qafiz (measure). The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle and states that if in a muzara'ah contract both parties stipulate that one of them will get a fixed qafiz, e.g., ten manns, and the rest is divided between them in partnership, then the contract becomes invalid because stipulating a fixed qafiz leads to ending the partnership, since it is possible that the produce is only that much and there is no remainder, which would cause dispute and discord. Every condition that causes partnership to end invalidates the contract, so stipulating a fixed qafiz invalidates it. Except that the fatwa is on their saying (i.e., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the permissibility of muzara'ah due to people's need and the emergence of public practice with it from the time of the Prophet (PBUH) to our day without rejection, and qiyas is abandoned due to public practice as in istisna'. Al-Binaayah: Book of Muzara'ah, vol. 1 p. 481; Al-Hidayah: Book of Muzara'ah, vol. 4 p. 426. And if they stipulate for one of them specific qafizes, they are void because that condition cuts off the partnership, as the land may produce only that amount.

Commentary
This passage presents a second principle regarding muzara'ah: any condition that terminates the profit-sharing partnership (e.g., guaranteeing a fixed quantity of produce to one party) invalidates the contract. The reasoning is that such a condition (shart ta'yin qafiz) potentially severs the partnership entirely if the crop yields only that fixed amount, leaving nothing to be shared. This leads to dispute (tanazu') and is thus prohibited. The commentator explains that the contract itself (without such conditions) is permissible according to the Sahibayn, as established previously. The Arabic excerpt from Al-Binaayah and Al-Hidayah reinforces that stipulating fixed qafizes is void (batilah) because it cuts off the partnership. The phrase 'illa an al-fatwa 'ala qawlihima' reaffirms that the fatwa is on the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the basic permissibility of muzara'ah, which is based on public practice overriding qiyas.

الشَّرُكَةُ (al-sharika): Partnership; in muzara'ah, the sharing of produce between landowner and worker based on their agreement.
قفيز (qafiz): A dry measure used for crops; here referring to a fixed quantity of produce guaranteed to one party.
باطل (batil): Void; a contract that has no legal effect and must be annulled.
مفتی بہ (mufta bihi): The relied-upon opinion for issuing fatwas; the operative ruling in the madhhab.

8 min
Stipulations Terminating Partnership Invalidate Muzara'ah
Legal principle and intra-Hanafi dispute on stipulations in sharecropping contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding stipulations that cut off partnership in muzara'ah and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A stipulation that severs partnership voids the contract — except where custom saves it.

This lesson presents a key legal principle (qawida) governing sharecropping (muzara'ah) in Hanafi fiqh. The principle states that any stipulation in a muzara'ah contract that terminates the partnership between landowner and worker renders the contract void. However, there is an important intra-Hanafi disagreement: while Imam Abu Hanifa held that such stipulations invalidate the contract, the relied-upon fatwa follows the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who uphold the contract's validity due to widespread custom (ta'amul) and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qawida
The passage begins with a legal maxim: 'The stipulation of something that cuts off partnership invalidates muzara'ah.' Recognize this as a general rule governing sharecropping contracts.
The key term is 'shirkah' (partnership) – the contract requires both parties to share in the crop proportionally.
2
Understand the Example
The example given is stipulating a fixed quantity (e.g., ten mans) for one party, with the remainder shared. This severs partnership because if the crop yields only that amount, the other party gets nothing, leading to dispute.
This uncertainty (gharar) is the basis for the rule.
3
Note the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that such conditions make the contract void (fasid). However, his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, hold that the contract remains valid due to the widespread practice of the ummah and necessity.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the Proof of the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn rely on istihsan (juristic preference) based on ta'amul (established custom). They argue that qiyas is abandoned in favor of custom, just as in the contract of istisna' (manufacturing order).
The proof is that the practice of the ummah from the Prophet's time onward has been to allow such conditions without rejection.
5
Recognize the Mufta Bihi
The author of Al-Hidayah states: 'But the fatwa is on their view' (إلا أن الفتوى على قولهما). Thus, the operative ruling is that the contract is valid despite such stipulations.
This shift in fatwa reflects the importance of custom in mu'amalat (transactions).

(15) The stipulation of something that cuts off partnership invalidates muzara'ah. Translation: Muzara'ah becomes void by conditions that terminate partnership. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that the contract of muzara'ah becomes void by any condition that cuts off partnership, for example stipulating a specific qafiz. Commentary
This lesson presents a core principle of Hanafi fiqh regarding sharecropping (muzara'ah). The author states a qawida (legal maxim): any stipulation in a muzara'ah contract that severs the partnership between the landowner and the worker renders the contract void. The example given is stipulating a fixed quantity of produce (e.g., ten mans) for one party, with the remainder to be shared. They argue that qiyas (analogy) can be abandoned in favor of established custom, just as in the contract of istisna' (manufacturing order). Thus, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that such stipulations do not invalidate the contract. The passage ends with an excerpt from Al-Hidayah explaining why the stipulation is problematic: the land might not yield more than the specified amount, cutting off partnership.

مزارعة (muzāra'ah): A sharecropping contract where the landowner provides land and the worker provides labor, with the crop shared according to agreement.
قفيز (qafīz): A fixed measure of grain; here, a specific quantity of produce stipulated for one party.
شركة (shirkah): Partnership in the profits from the land; the contract requires both parties to share in the crop proportionally.
تعامل (ta'āmul): The established practice of the people, which can overrule qiyas in contractual matters.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a stipulation that cuts off partnership in a muzara'ah contract is void in principle, but the fatwa follows the Sahibayn who uphold validity due to custom (ta'amul).

Postponement is Easier Than Nullification in Muzara'ah
Ruling with proof: applying a legal maxim to protect the worker's crop.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-ta'khīr ahwan min al-ibtāl' to resolve conflicts between a worker's right to a standing crop and creditors' claims in a muzara'ah contract.

Core Idea
Delay creditors, don't destroy the worker's crop — postponement is the lesser harm.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi ruling in agricultural partnerships. When a landowner falls into debt before harvest, creditors may demand the sale of the land. But what if that sale would destroy the worker's right to the crop? The Hanafi school invokes a powerful maxim: postponing a right is easier than nullifying it. You will see how the judge weighs the two harms and chooses the lesser one, protecting the worker's livelihood without permanently denying the creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting rights
In the scenario, two parties have claims: the worker (muzari') has a right to the standing crop under the muzara'ah contract, and the creditors (ghurama') have a right to payment from the landowner's assets, including the land. Both cannot be fully satisfied simultaneously.
The worker's right is attached to the crop itself (a 'ayn), whereas the creditors' right is a debt (dayn) on the landowner.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The ruling from Al-Hidayah is clear: the land shall not be sold for the debt until the crop is harvested (lā tubā' al-arḍ bil-dayn ḥattā yustaḥṣad al-zar'). This is a prohibition on immediate sale.
It is not a mere recommendation; it is a binding judicial order.
3
Locate the evidence
The evidence is the legal maxim 'al-ta'khīr ahwan min al-ibtāl' and the underlying principle that when two harms conflict, the greater harm is avoided by committing the lesser harm (idhā ta'āraḍat mafsadatān ru'īa a'ẓamuhumā ḍararan bi-irtikāb akhaffihimā).
This is a rational (aqlī) proof based on harm analysis, not a revealed text.
4
Analyze the harm weighing
Compare the two harms: nullification of the worker's right (ibtāl) vs. postponement of the creditors' right (ta'khīr). Nullification destroys the worker's entire season of labor and the crop itself, a concrete loss. Postponement only delays the creditors' payment; they will eventually get paid after harvest.
The harm to the worker is greater because it involves a specific physical asset ('ayn).
5
Apply the macro-principle
Since nullification is the greater harm, the lesser harm (postponement) is chosen. Therefore, the land is not sold until harvest, even though creditors are inconvenienced.
This is a classic Hanafi application of harm prioritization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٦) اَلتَّأْخِيرُ أَهْوَنُ مِنَ الْإِبْطَال. ترجمہ: ( حق کو مؤخر کر نا باطل کرنے سے زیادہ آسان ہے۔ تشریح مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جہاں حق کو مؤخر کرنے سے حق کے بطلان سے بچا جاسکتا ہو تو وہاں تأخیر زیادہ بہتر اور آسان ہے، لہذا تاخیر کے پہلو کو اپنا یا جائے گانہ کہ ابطال کے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر مزارعت میں لی گئی زمین میں کھیتی اگ آئی اور ابھی تک وہ کائی نہیں گئی ہے اور مالک زمین پر قرض بھی آگیا تو زمین کو مالک کے قرضوں میں فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا جب تک کھیتی کاٹ نہ دی جائے ، کیونکہ کھیتی نہ کٹنے کی صورت میں قرض خواہوں کا حق مؤخر ہو جائے گا اور کھیتی کاٹنے کی صورت میں مزارع یعنی عامل کا حق باطل ہو جائے گا اور تاخیر ابطال سے اہون ہے اور یہ اصول ہے إِذَا تَعَارَضَ مَفْسَدَتَانِ رُوْعِيَ أَعْظَمُهُمَا ضَرَرًا بِارْتِكَابِ أَخَفِّهِمَا ، یعنی جب دوضرر جمع ہو جائیں تو اس وقت اخف اور اہوں کو اختیار کیا جاتا ہے، یہاں کھیتی کا نہ کا نا اہون ہے کاٹنے سے ، اس لیے اس کو تر جیح دی جائے گی۔ ( البناية : كتاب المزارعة، ج ۱ ص ۴۹۰) الهداية كتاب المزارعة، ج ۴ ص ۴۲۸ وَمَا إِذَا فَسَخَ وَقَدْ نَبَتَ الزَّرْعُ وَلَمْ يُسْتَحْصَدَ بَعْدُ، وَحُكْمُهُ أَنْ لَا تُبَاعَ الْأَرْضُ بِالدَّيْنِ حَتَّى يُسْتَحْصَدَ الزَّرْعُ لِأَنَّ فِى الْبَيْعِ إِبْطَالَ حَقَّ الْمُزَارِعِ، وَفِي التَّأْخِيرِ إِنْ كَانَ إِضْرَارًا بِالْغُرَمَاءِ لَكِنَّ التَّأْخِيرَ أَهْوَنُ مِنْ الْإِبْطَالِ. (العناية: كتاب المزارعة، ج ۹ ص ۴۷۵) وَلَوْ نَبَتَ الزَّرْعُ ، وَلَمْ يُسْتَحْصَدُ لَمْ تُبَعُ الأَرْضُ فِي الدَّيْنِ حَتَّى يُسْتَحْصَدَ الزَّرْعُ. (مجمع الضمانات كتاب المزارعة، ج ۲ ص۳۱۷)
(16) Postponement is easier than nullification. Translation: Postponing a right is easier than invalidating it. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that when a right can be preserved by postponement rather than cancellation, postponement is better and easier. Therefore, the aspect of postponement should be adopted, not cancellation. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if in a muzara'ah the crop has grown but not yet harvested, and the landowner incurs debt, the land will not be sold for the debt until the crop is harvested. Because if the crop is not harvested, the creditors' right is postponed; if the crop is harvested, the right of the muzari' (worker) is nullified. And postponement is easier than nullification. This is based on the principle: 'When two harms conflict, the greater harm is avoided by committing the lesser harm.' That is, when two harms come together, the easier and lighter is chosen. Here, not cutting the crop is lighter than cutting it, so it is given preference. (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Muzara'ah, vol. 1, p. 490) (Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Muzara'ah, vol. 4, p. 428) 'And if the contract is rescinded and the crop has grown but not yet harvested, the ruling is that the land is not sold for debt until the crop is harvested, because in selling there is nullification of the right of the muzari', and in postponement there is harm to the creditors, but postponement is easier than nullification.' (Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Muzara'ah, vol. 9, p. 475) 'And if the crop has grown and not yet been harvested, the land is not sold for debt until the crop is harvested.' (Majma' al-Damanat: Kitab al-Muzara'ah, vol. 2, p. 317)
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This lesson presents another important legal maxim from the Hanafi school: 'Postponement is easier than nullification' (al-ta'khīr ahwan min al-ibtāl). The author applies this to a scenario in muzara'ah where the crop is standing but unharvested, and the landowner falls into debt. The question arises: can the creditors force the sale of the land to recover the debt? The ruling is that the land cannot be sold until the crop is harvested, because selling the land would nullify the worker's right to the crop (as the land changes hands), while postponing the creditors' claim only delays their right. Since both actions cause harm—nullification of the worker's right vs. delay to creditors—the lesser harm (postponement) is chosen. The underlying principle is 'When two harms conflict, the greater harm is avoided by committing the lesser harm' (iḏā ta'āraḍat mafsadatān ru'īa a'ẓamuhumā ḍararan bi-irtikāb akhaffihimā). This is a classic example of weighing harms in Hanafi fiqh. The passage quotes from Al-Hidayah, Al-'Inayah, and Majma' al-Damanat to reinforce the rule.

Key Terms
تأخير (ta'khīr): Deferring a right or obligation to a later time; postponement.
إبطال (ibtāl): Nullification or cancellation of a right or contract.
غرماء (ghuramā'): Creditors to whom the landowner owes debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario described, why is the land not sold for debt until the crop is harvested?
A) Because the crop is still growing and cannot be sold.
B) Because selling the land would nullify the worker's right, which is worse than postponing the creditors' claim.
C) Because the creditors have no legal right to the land.
D) Because the landowner must first harvest the crop to pay the debt.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'postponement is easier than nullification' protects the worker's right to a standing crop by prioritizing delay of creditors over nullification of the worker's right.

Texts Are ‘Illah-Based: Musaqat Permissibility Extended by Cause
Explaining the Hanafi principle that texts are caused, applied to musaqat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that texts are maʿlūla (caused) and apply it to extend the ruling of musāqāt beyond the explicit texts.

Core Idea
The ruling in a text is general if its cause (illah) is general — need makes musaqat permissible on all trees.

This lesson unpacks a foundational Hanafi usuli principle: that legal texts are inherently grounded in effective causes (ʿilal). The author of al-Hidāyah uses this principle to resolve a well-known dispute with the Shāfiʿī school over the scope of the musāqāt contract. Understanding this principle will show you how the Ḥanafīs extend rulings beyond literal texts, using the illah as a bridge.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
First, identify what the Ḥanafī school holds regarding the permissibility of musāqāt. The passage states that musāqāt is allowed for all trees, vines, and vegetables, not just those mentioned in the hadith.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Recognize that Imām al-Shāfiʿī restricts musāqāt to date palms and grapevines. His evidence is the hadith of Khaybar, which only mentions those two.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Ḥanafīs counter with the principle that texts are caused. Even if the text mentions only two cases, the underlying cause (ʿilla) – general human need – is present in all similar contracts, so the ruling applies universally.
The principle 'al-aṣl fī al-nuṣūṣ an takūna maʿlūla' is the key argument.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Ḥanafī law, a musāqāt contract on mango or apple trees is valid; under Shāfiʿī law, it is not. This shows the real-world impact of the usuli difference.

تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جتنے بھی نصوص میں ان میں اصل یہ ہے کہ وہ معلول بالعلت ہوں تا کہ اس علت کی بنیاد پر غیر منصوص میں حکم لگایا جائے ، لہذا احناف
کے ہاں نصوص معلول بالعلت متعدیہ ہوتے ہیں۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ احناف کے نزدیک مساقاة کھجور کے درختوں میں انگور کی بیلوں میں اور دیگر اشجار اور سبزیوں میں جائز ہے، جبکہ امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک صرف انگور کے بیلوں اور کھجور کے درختوں میں جائز ہے، امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وہ فرماتے ہیں نص صرف ان دو کے بارے میں وارد ہوئی ہے، احناف کی طرف سے امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کی دلیل کا جواب مذکورہ اصول ہے کہ اگر ہم تسلیم بھی کر لیں کہ نص صرف ان دو کے بارے میں وارد ہے، لیکن نصوص میں اصل یہ ہے کہ وہ معلول بالعلت ہو، لہذا ان دو میں جواز کی جو علت ہے وہ دیگر سبزیوں میں پائی جاتی ہے اور وہ علت ضرورت وحاجت ہے، اور علت میں عموم تقاضا کرتا ہے حکم میں عموم کا ، لہذا عات یعنی ضرورت و حاجت کی وجہ سے حکم میں عموم ہوگا اور دیگر چیزوں میں بھی مساقات جائز ہوگا۔

وَتَجُوزُ الْمُسَاقَاةُ فِى النَّخِيلِ وَالشَّجَرِ) هَذَا بَيَانُ مَا تَجْرِي فِيهِ الْمُسَاقَاةُ وَمَا لَا تَجْرِى فِيهِ، وَخَصَّصَ الشَّافِعِيُّ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ جَوَازَهَا بِمَا وَرَدَ فِيهِ الْأَثَرُ مِنْ حَدِيثِ خَيْبَرَ وَكَانَ فِي النَّخْلِ وَالْكَرَمِ وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْجَوَاز لِلْحَاجَةِ وَقَدْ عَمَّتْ وَعُمُومُ الْعِلَّةِ
(1) The principle regarding texts is that they are caused. Translation: The principle about texts is that they are caused. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that all texts (nusūṣ) are, in principle, caused by an effective cause (ʿilla) so that based on that cause a ruling can be applied to untexted cases. Therefore, according to the Ḥanafīs, texts are caused by a transitive cause (ʿilla mutaʿaddiya). The author of al-Hidāyah mentions this principle to explain that according to the Ḥanafīs, musāqāt is permissible for date palms, grapevines, and other trees and vegetables. While Imam al-Shāfiʿī (may Allah have mercy on him) holds that it is only permissible for grapevines and date palms. Imam al-Shāfiʿī's evidence is that the text (hadith) only came concerning these two. The Ḥanafī response to Imam al-Shāfiʿī's evidence is the mentioned principle: even if we concede that the text only came concerning these two, the principle regarding texts is that they are caused. Therefore, the cause (ʿilla) for permissibility in these two is found in other vegetables, and that cause is need (ḍarūra wa ḥāja), and generality of the cause demands generality of the ruling. Thus, because of the cause (need), the ruling will be general, and musāqāt will be permissible in other things as well. Al-Hidāyah, Kitāb al-Musāqāt, vol. 4 p. 431: 'And musāqāt is permissible for date palms and trees. This is an explanation of what musāqāt applies to and what it does not. Al-Shāfiʿī (may Allah have mercy on him) restricted its permissibility to what is reported in the hadith of Khaybar, which was about date palms and grapevines. And our evidence is that permissibility is due to need, and need is general, and the generality of the cause...'
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents another key Hanafi usuli principle: 'The principle regarding texts is that they are caused (maʿlūla).' The author first states the maxim, then provides its Urdu translation and explanation. The meaning is that all revealed texts (nusūṣ) inherently have an effective cause (ʿilla) underlying their rulings. This allows the Hanafi jurist to extend the ruling to analogous cases not explicitly mentioned in the text, because the ʿilla is considered transitive (mutaʿaddiya). The author then applies this principle to the issue of musāqāt (a contract for watering and tending trees in exchange for a share of the fruit). The Hanafi school permits musāqāt not only for date palms and grapevines (as mentioned in the hadith of Khaybar) but also for all other trees and vegetables. Imām al-Shāfiʿī restricts it to date palms and grapevines based on the text. The Hanafi response is that even if the text explicitly mentions only those two, the effective cause (ʿilla) for permissibility is the general human need (hāja wa ḍarūra) for such contracts, which applies to all trees and crops. Since the ʿilla is general, the ruling is general. This illustrates the Hanafi method of qiyās based on ʿilla and their principle that texts are ʿilla-based. The passage ends with a partial quote from al-Hidāyah affirming this reasoning.

Key Terms
نصوص معلولة (nusūṣ maʿlūla): Texts that have an effective cause (ʿilla) behind their rulings, allowing analogy
مساقاة (musāqāt): A contract where one party tends trees or vines in exchange for a share of the fruit
علة (ʿilla): The effective cause or ratio legis that triggers a ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi reasoning for permitting musaqat on all trees and not just date palms and grapevines?
A) They reject the hadith of Khaybar
B) They consider the cause (need) to be general
C) They follow consensus
D) They analogize to sale contracts

Key Takeaway
Texts are caused by ʿillal; if the cause is general, the ruling extends to all cases sharing that cause.

The Principle of Ta'lil in Musaqah: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Explains the Hanafi principle that texts are 'illah-based, applied to Musaqah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits Musaqah for all plants and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Texts are 'illah-based; the 'illah of need makes Musaqah permissible for all plants, not just those mentioned in the hadith.

This lesson examines a fundamental Hanafi usul principle—that legal texts are presumed to be 'illah-based—and applies it to the contract of Musaqah (sharecropping). The Hanafi school extends Musaqah to all trees and vegetables, while Imam al-Shafi'i restricts it to date palms and grapevines based on the explicit hadith of Khaybar. Understanding how the Hanafis derive this ruling reveals the central role of 'illah (effective cause) in their jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its proof
Identify that Hanafis permit Musaqah in all trees, vines, and vegetables. Their proof: the 'illah of general need (hajah).
The principle 'al-asl fi al-nusus an takuna ma'lulah' is invoked.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i restricts Musaqah to date palms and grapevines based on the explicit wording of the Khaybar hadith.
The Shafi'i school considers the text's specificity binding.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that texts are subject to ta'lil, so the ruling extends wherever the 'illah (need) is found, even if the text mentions only specific plants.
They also note that the people of Khaybar worked with other trees, indicating the 'illah is not restricted.
4
Compare methodological approaches
Hanafis require a transgressive 'illah (muta'addiyah) that can move to new cases; Shafi'is allow non-transgressive 'illah (qasirah) that stays within the text.
This difference in usul leads to divergent rulings.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Hanafis allow Musaqah for eggplant, cucumber, etc.; Shafi'is only for date palms and grapevines.
This affects agricultural contracts in practice.

Source Text

كتاب المساقاة
( ۱ ) اَلْأَصْلُ فِي النُّصُوصِ أَنْ تَكُونَ مَعْلُولَةً. ترجمہ: نصوص میں اصل یہ ہے کہ وہ معلول ہو۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جتنے بھی نصوص میں ان میں اصل یہ ہے کہ وہ معلول بالعلت ہوں تا کہ اس علت کی بنیاد پر غیر منصوص میں حکم لگایا جائے ، لہذا احناف کے ہاں نصوص معلول بالعلت متعدیہ ہوتے ہیں۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ احناف کے نزدیک مساقاة کھجور کے درختوں میں انگور کی بیلوں میں اور دیگر اشجار اور سبزیوں میں جائز ہے، جبکہ امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک صرف انگور کے بیلوں اور کھجور کے درختوں میں جائز ہے، امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وہ فرماتے ہیں نص صرف ان دو کے بارے میں وارد ہوئی ہے، احناف کی طرف سے امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کی دلیل کا جواب مذکورہ اصول ہے کہ اگر ہم تسلیم بھی کر لیں کہ نص صرف ان دو کے بارے میں وارد ہے، لیکن نصوص میں اصل یہ ہے کہ وہ معلول بالعلت ہو، لہذا ان دو میں جواز کی جو علت ہے وہ دیگر سبزیوں میں پائی جاتی ہے اور وہ علت ضرورت وحاجت ہے، اور علت میں عموم تقاضا کرتا ہے حکم میں عموم کا ، لہذا عات یعنی ضرورت و حاجت کی وجہ سے حکم میں عموم ہوگا اور دیگر چیزوں میں بھی مساقات جائز ہوگا۔
الهداية كتاب المساقاة، ج ۴ ص ۴۳۱
وَتَجُوزُ الْمُسَاقَاةُ فِى النَّخِيلِ وَالشَّجَرِ) هَذَا بَيَانُ مَا تَجْرِي فِيهِ الْمُسَاقَاةُ وَمَا لَا تَجْرِى فِيهِ، وَخَصَّصَ الشَّافِعِيُّ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ جَوَازَهَا بِمَا وَرَدَ فِيهِ الْأَثَرُ مِنْ حَدِيثِ خَيْبَرَ وَكَانَ فِي النَّخْلِ وَالْكَرَمِ وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْجَوَاز لِلْحَاجَةِ وَقَدْ عَمَّتْ وَعُمُومُ الْعِلَّةِ يَقْتَضِى عُمُومَ الْحُكْمِ وَلَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ أَثَرَ خَيْبَرَ خَصَّهُمَا لِأَنَّ أَهْلَهَا يَعْمَلُونَ فِي الْأَشْجَارِ وَالرِّطَابِ أَيْضًا، وَلَئِنْ سَلَّمْنَا ذَلِكَ لَكِنَّ الْأَصْلَ فِي النُّصُوصِ التَّعْلِيلُ لَا سِيَّمَا عَلَى أَصْلِهِ، فَإِنَّ بَابَهُ عِنْدَهُ أَوْسَعُ لِأَنَّهُ يَرَى التَّعْلِيلَ بِالْعِلَّةِ الْقَاصِرَةِ وَبِكُلِّ وَصْفِ قَامَ دَلِيلُ التَّمُسِيرِ عَلَى كَوْنِهِ جَامِعًا بَيْنَ الْأَوْصَافِ، وَأَمَّا نَحْنُ فَإِنَّا لَا نُجَوِّزُهُ بِالْعِلَّةِ الْقَاصِرَةِ، وَيُشْتَرَطُ قِيَامُ الدَّلِيلِ هَذَا النَّصَّ بِعَيْنِهِ مَعْلُولٌ وَمَوْضِعُهُ أُصُولُ الْفِقْهِ. (العناية: كتاب المساقاة، ج ۹ ص ۴۸۰، ۴۸۱)
وتجوز المساقاة في النخل والشجر والكرم والرطاب) بكسر الراء كقصاع جمع رطبة بالفتح كقصعة القضيب ما دام رطبا كما في الصحاح، وهي المسماة في بلادنا بالقصة، والمراد هنا جميع البقول كما في الدر وأصول الباذنجان)، لأن الجواز للحاجة وهى تعم الجميع.
(اللباب فى شرح الكتاب: كتاب المساقاة، ج ۲ ص ۲۳۴)
Book of Musaqah. (1) The principle in texts is that they are caused (ma'lulah). Translation: The principle in texts is that they are caused. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that all texts, the principle in them is that they are caused by an effective cause ('illah) so that based on that 'illah a ruling can be applied to non-textual matters. Therefore, according to the Hanafis, texts are caused by a transgressive 'illah. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that according to the Hanafis, Musaqah is permissible in date palms, grapevines, and other trees and vegetables, while according to Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, it is only permissible in grapevines and date palms. Imam al-Shafi'i's evidence is that the text only came concerning these two. The response from the Hanafis to Imam al-Shafi'i's evidence is the mentioned principle: even if we accept that the text only came concerning these two, the principle in texts is that they are caused. Therefore, the cause of permissibility in these two is found in other vegetables, and that cause is necessity and need. And generality in the cause necessitates generality in the ruling. So due to necessity and need, the ruling will be general and Musaqah will be permissible in other things as well. Al-Hidayah, Book of Musaqah, vol. 4, p. 431: 'And Musaqah is permissible in date palms and trees. This is an explanation of what Musaqah applies to and what it does not apply to. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, restricted its permissibility to what has been reported in the narration of Khaybar, which was about date palms and grapevines. Our evidence is that permissibility is due to need, and it is general, and the generality of the 'illah necessitates generality of the ruling. We do not accept that the Khaybar narration specifically limited them, because its people also work with trees and fresh produce. And even if we accept that, the principle in texts is that they are subject to ta'lil (causation), especially according to his principle, for his scope is wider because he permits ta'lil with a non-transgressive 'illah (al-'illah al-qasirah) and with every description for which evidence of suitability (tamsir) exists that it is a comprehensive collection of descriptions. As for us, we do not permit it with a non-transgressive 'illah; rather, the evidence must be established. This same text is caused, and its place is Usul al-Fiqh.' (Al-'Inayah: Book of Musaqah, vol. 9, pp. 480-481) 'And Musaqah is permissible in date palms, trees, grapevines, and fresh produce (al-ratab) - with kasr of the ra' like qisa', plural of ratbah with fatha like qas'ah: a branch as long as it is fresh, as in al-Sihah. It is called in our land qissah. Here, it means all vegetables, as in al-Durr and the roots of eggplants, because permissibility is due to need, and it encompasses all.' (Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab: Book of Musaqah, vol. 2, p. 234)
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the scope of Musaqah (a sharecropping contract for trees and vines). The author begins by stating a fundamental usul principle: 'The original principle in texts (nusus) is that they are 'illah-based (ma'lulah).' This means that whenever a legal ruling is found in a primary text (Qur'an or Sunnah), it is understood to be based on an underlying cause ('illah) that can be extended to other similar cases. The Hanafi school holds that the 'illah is the general need (hajah) of people to engage in Musaqah for various plants, not just date palms and grapevines. Therefore, Musaqah is permissible in all trees, vines, vegetables, etc. Imam al-Shafi'i restricts it to date palms and grapevines because the explicit hadith from Khaybar only mentioned these two. The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) Even if the hadith specifically mentions those two, the practice of the people of Khaybar included other trees, indicating that the 'illah is not restricted. (2) The principle of ta'lil requires that the ruling be extended wherever the 'illah is found. The author then distinguishes between the Hanafi methodology and that of al-Shafi'i: al-Shafi'i allows ta'lil with a non-transgressive 'illah (al-'illah al-qasirah), which stays within the text, while the Hanafis require a transgressive 'illah that can be applied to new cases. The final Arabic text from Al-Lubab confirms that Musaqah includes all fresh produce and vegetables because the need is general.

Key Terms
نص (nass): A primary text (Qur'an or Sunnah) that establishes a ruling.
علت ('illah): The effective cause or rationale behind a legal ruling, deduced from the text.
مساقاة (musaqah): A sharecropping contract where the owner of trees/vines gives them to a cultivator in exchange for a share of the produce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis permit Musaqah in all types of trees and vegetables, unlike Imam al-Shafi'i?
A) Because they reject the hadith of Khaybar.
B) Because they consider the 'illah of need to be general and apply to all plants.
C) Because there is a different hadith explicitly permitting all plants.
D) Because they analogize it to Ijara.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that texts are 'illah-based allows Musaqah for all plants, while Shafi'is restrict to explicit texts.

Ruling on Deliberately Omitting Tasmiyah in Slaughter
States that deliberate omission of tasmiyah is not subject to ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on deliberately omitting tasmiyah during slaughter and explain why it cannot be overridden by ijtihad or judicial ruling.

Core Idea
Deliberately omitting tasmiyah makes meat haram by ijma' — no judge or jurist can contradict it.

This passage from Al-Hidayah and Majma' al-Anhur presents a definitive ruling on slaughter. It states that deliberately omitting the utterance of 'Bismillah' renders the animal unlawful, and this issue is not open to ijtihad because it is established by consensus. The passage also addresses the nullity of any judicial ruling that permits the sale of such meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being judged? The act of deliberately omitting tasmiyah (saying 'Bismillah') when slaughtering an animal. The ruling: the meat becomes haram.
The Arabic term 'matruk al-tasmiyah' refers specifically to an animal slaughtered without tasmiyah.
2
Determine the evidentiary basis
The passage bases this ruling on ijma' (consensus of the Companions and scholars). The phrase 'la yasa'u fihi al-ijtihad' indicates that the proof is qat'i (definitive).
Ijma' is the third source of law after Quran and Sunnah, and in Hanafi usul it is a binding proof.
3
Explain why it is not subject to ijtihad
Ijtihad is only allowed in matters where the evidence is zanni (speculative). Since ijma' is qat'i, no legitimate ijtihad can contradict it. This means any contrary opinion is invalid.
This principle protects established rulings from arbitrary reinterpretation.
4
Examine the consequence on a judicial ruling
If a judge (qadi) rules that the sale of such meat is permissible, that ruling is void (la yanfudh) and must not be executed. Judicial authority does not override definitive textual evidence.
This reinforces the supremacy of shari'ah over judicial discretion.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that deliberately omitting tasmiyah does not render the meat haram, only makruh. However, the Hanafi position is stricter due to their understanding of the evidence and reliance on ijma'.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate and stems from different usul principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَالْمَشَايِخُ عَلَى أَنَّ مَتْرُوكَ التَّسْمِيَةِ عَامِدًا لَا يَسَعُ فِيهِ الاجْتِهَادُ وَلَوْ قَضَى الْقَاضِي بِجَوَازِ بَيْعِهِ لَا يَنْفُذُ (مجمع الأنهر : كتاب الذبائح، ج۲ ص ۵۰۸) الهداية كتاب الذبائح، ج ۴، ص ۴۳۷
Abu Yusuf and the mashayikh said: The one who deliberately omits the tasmiyah is not subject to ijtihad, and if a judge passes a ruling permitting its sale, it is not executed. (Majma' al-Anhur: Kitab al-Dhaba'ih, vol. 2 p. 508; Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Dhaba'ih, vol. 4 p. 437)
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage states a definitive ruling within the Hanafi school: deliberately omitting the tasmiyah (saying 'Bismillah') when slaughtering renders the animal unlawful, and this issue is not open to ijtihad because it is established by consensus (ijma'). Therefore, no qualified jurist may hold a contrary opinion. If a judge mistakenly or deliberately rules that the sale of such meat is permissible, that ruling is null and void and must not be enforced. The text quotes Abu Yusuf and later mashayikh (senior scholars) confirming this, and references two authoritative books: Majma' al-Anhur and Al-Hidayah. The principle is that ijma' is the strongest evidence, and any judicial decision contradicting the Book, Sunnah, or Ijma' is to be overturned.

Key Terms
متروك التسمية (matruk al-tasmiyah): Refers to an animal slaughtered while deliberately omitting the utterance of 'Bismillah'.
لا يَسَعُ فيه الاجتهاد (la yasa'u fihi al-ijtihad): Means that the ruling is so definitively established that no legitimate ijtihad can contradict it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a judge rules that the sale of meat from an animal slaughtered without tasmiyah is permissible?
A) The ruling is valid and must be enforced.
B) The ruling is invalid and not executed because it contradicts ijma'.
C) The ruling is valid but only for that particular case.
D) The judge is to be removed from office.

Key Takeaway
Deliberately omitting tasmiyah during slaughter makes the meat haram by consensus, and any judicial ruling contrary to this is void.

The Qa'idah of Objective Over Cause: Slaughter with Nail, Tooth, and Horn
Applies a qa'idah to permit slaughter with alternative tools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on slaughter with a detached nail, tooth, or horn, and apply the qa'idah 'la yubala bi-ikhtilaf al-sabab' to justify its permissibility.

Core Idea
Once blood flows, the tool doesn't matter — any cutting implement makes slaughter valid.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi qa'idah that focuses on outcomes rather than means. The passage from Al-Hidayah discusses whether a detached nail, tooth, or horn can be used for slaughter. The answer is yes, but with a dislike (karahah). The reasoning is a beautiful application of the maxim: 'No attention is paid to the difference of the cause when the objective is achieved.' Understanding this principle will help you evaluate similar cases where the means vary but the end is the same.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is slaughtering an animal with a detached nail, tooth, or horn. The ruling in the Hanafi school is that it is permissible (jā'iz) but makruh tanzihi (disliked).
The ruling applies only when these tools are detached from a living animal. If attached, it is different.
2
Understand the qa'idah
The maxim 'لَا يُبَالَى بِاخْتِلافِ السَّبَبِ عِندَ حُصُولِ الْمَقْصُودِ' means that once the objective is achieved, the difference in the cause or means is irrelevant. Here the objective is blood flow (ihrar al-dam).
3
Apply the qa'idah to the case
Since the nail, tooth, or horn is a cutting tool (āla jāriḥa) that causes blood to flow, it achieves the objective of slaughter. Therefore, the slaughter is valid. The qa'idah overrides any objection based on the tool's origin.
The commentary from Al-Inayah emphasizes that these tools are analogous to a sharpened stone or iron.
4
Evaluate the karahah
The ruling includes karahah tanzihiyyah because using these tools may cause more pain to the animal. Using a sharp knife is better, but the slaughter remains valid.
The karahah does not affect validity; it only indicates that a better alternative exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا يُبَالَى بِاخْتِلافِ السَّبَبِ عِندَ حُصُولِ الْمَقْصُودِ. قَالَ وَيَجُوزُ الذَّبْحُ بِالظُّفْرِ وَالْقَرْنِ وَالسِّنِّ إِلَخُ الذَّبْحُ بِالظُّفْرِ وَالْقَرْنِ وَالسِّنِّ الْمَنْزُوعَةِ جَائِزٌ مَكْرُوهُ، وَأَكُلُ النَّبِيحِ بِهَا لَا بَأْسَ بِهِ ... وَقَوْلُهُ وَلَأَنَّهُ آلَةٌ جَارِحَةٌ) جَوَابٌ عَنْ دَلِيلِهِ الْمَعْقُولِ. وَتَقْرِيرُهُ: إِنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ إِنْهَارَ الدَّمِ بِالظُّفْرِ وَالسِّنَّ الْمَسْرُوعَيْنِ غَيْرُ مَشْرُوعٍ ، فَإِنَّهُ أَى كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا آلَةٌ جَارِحَةٌ يَحْصُلُ بِهَا الْمَقْصُودُ وَهُوَ إِخْرَاجُ الدَّمِ فَصَارَ كَاللِّيطَةِ وَالْحَجَرِ وَالْحَدِيدِ وَالسِّكِّينِ الكليل (العناية: كتاب الذبائح، ج ۹ ص ۴۹۵،۴۹۴) الهداية كتاب الذبائح، ج ۴ ص ۴۳۷
No attention is paid to the difference of the cause when the objective is achieved. He said: Slaughter with the nail, horn, and tooth is permissible. Slaughter with the detached nail, horn, and tooth is permissible but makruh, and eating the animal slaughtered with them is not objectionable... And his statement 'because it is a cutting tool' is a response to the rational proof. Its explanation: We do not concede that causing blood to flow with the detached nail and tooth is not prescribed, for each of them is a cutting tool by which the objective is achieved, i.e., the extraction of blood, so it becomes like a sharpened stone, a stone, iron, and a dull knife. (Al-Inayah: Kitab al-Dhaba'ih, vol. 9 pp. 494-495; Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Dhaba'ih, vol. 4 p. 437)
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim (qa'idah) and applies it to the permissibility of slaughtering with a detached nail, tooth, or horn. The maxim states that once the intended purpose is realized, variation in the means or cause is irrelevant. In slaughter, the objective is to cause blood to flow out (ihrar al-dam). If a detached nail, tooth, or horn is sharp enough to cut the throat and veins, it achieves the same objective as a knife. Hence, slaughter is valid. However, it is makruh tanzihi (disliked) because it may cause more pain to the animal. The commentary from Al-Inayah explains that these items are cutting tools (ala jarihah) and thus analogous to a sharp-edged stone or a dull knife, which are valid. The author responds to an imagined objection that such tools are not prescribed; he says they are prescribed because they cut and let blood flow.

Key Terms
آلة جارحة (āla jāriḥa): A cutting tool that can sever the throat and cause blood to flow.
مكروه (makrūh): Here it means makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful), because a better alternative (using a knife) exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the qa'idah, why is slaughter with a detached horn and tooth permissible?
A) Because the horn and tooth are pure.
B) Because they cause blood to flow just like a knife.
C) Because the Prophet used them.
D) Because they are customary.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes the objective of blood flow over the material of the tool, allowing any cutting implement for valid slaughter.

Unnecessary Pain in Slaughter is Makruh
Qa'idah on extra pain during slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on causing extra pain during slaughter and apply it to specific actions such as cutting the spinal cord or severing the head.

Core Idea
Extra pain during slaughter is makruh but does not make the meat haram.

This lesson introduces a key qa'idah from Al-Hidayah regarding kindness to animals during slaughter. The principle is that any act causing unnecessary pain is disliked, yet the slaughter remains valid. Understanding this distinction between the ruling of the act and the permissibility of the meat is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the qa'idah
Identify the general principle: any action during slaughter that adds unnecessary pain is makruh (disliked).
The qa'idah is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
Identify the specific applications
Note the two examples given: cutting the spinal cord (nukha') and completely severing the head before the animal dies.
These are concrete cases where extra pain occurs.
3
Understand the ruling (hukm)
The ruling is makruh tanzihi—disliked but not sinful. The act is not haram and does not affect the validity of the slaughter.
4
Note the effect on the meat
The text explicitly states that the slaughtered animal is lawful to eat. The disliked act does not make the meat impermissible.
Common error: thinking makruh implies haram.
5
Connect to the broader principle
This ruling reflects Islamic compassion towards animals. The goal is to minimize suffering while ensuring effective slaughter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَا فِيْهِ زِيَادَةً إِيَّلَام لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ فِي الذَّبْحِ مَكْرُوهُ. قوله: (ومن بلغ بالسكين النخاع أو قطع الرأس كره له ذلك وتؤكل ذبيحته النخاع عرق أبيض فى عظم الرقبة
Whatever involves additional pain that is not needed in slaughter is makruh. His statement: (Whoever reaches the spinal cord with the knife or cuts off the head, that is disliked for him, yet his slaughtered animal is eaten. The spinal cord is a white vein in the bone of the neck).
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents another qa'idah: any act during slaughter that causes unnecessary extra pain to the animal is makruh (disliked). The author then applies it to two specific actions: (1) cutting through the spinal cord (nukha') which is a white nerve running inside the neck bone, and (2) completely severing the head before the animal has died. Both actions cause additional suffering beyond what is required for proper slaughter. However, the slaughter itself remains valid and the meat is halal to eat; the prohibition is one of tanzih (better to avoid). The underlying principle is compassion towards animals in Islamic law. The example from the text shows that even though the act is disliked, it does not affect the permissibility of consuming the meat.

Key Terms
النخاع (al-nukha'): The white nerve (spinal cord) inside the neck bone; cutting it causes immediate paralysis but the animal may still be alive.
زيادة إيلام (ziyada ilam): An increase in pain that goes beyond what is necessary for effective slaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on cutting the spinal cord during slaughter according to the passage?
A) It is haram and the meat cannot be eaten.
B) It is makruh but the meat is halal.
C) It is permissible without dislike.
D) It invalidates the slaughter.

Key Takeaway
Causing unnecessary pain during slaughter is makruh, but the meat remains halal; the ruling is about the act, not the permissibility of consumption.
